As for the second [condition], namely the stipulation of separation, you know that it is the view of Muhammad, so waqf of undivided property is not valid. Abu Yusuf said: It is permissible, and it is based on the first condition because division is part of delivery; whoever stipulates it does not permit waqf of undivided property, and whoever does not stipulate it permits it. The dispute concerns what is capable of division. As for what is not capable of division, it is valid by consensus, by analogy according to Muhammad with gift and direct alms, except for a mosque and cemetery, for it is not completed with common ownership even in what is not capable of division according to Abu Yusuf as well, because remaining partnership prevents exclusivity for Allah, and because alternating use in this is extremely repugnant—burying dead one year and planting the next, praying in it at one time and using it as a stable at another—unlike waqf because exploitation is possible. The conclusion: waqf of undivided property as a mosque or cemetery is absolutely invalid by consensus; in other cases, if it is not capable of division, it is valid by consensus. The dispute is over what is capable of division. Whoever takes Abu Yusuf's view in its issuance alone, the mashayikh of Balkh take his view in this; whoever takes Muhammad's view on delivery, the mashayikh of Bukhara take his view on waqf of undivided property. In al-Khulasa from Kitab al-Ijara and al-Waqf it is stated that the fatwa is on Muhammad's view regarding waqf of undivided property, and similarly in al-Bazaziyya, al-Walwalijiyya, and Sharh al-Majma' by Ibn al-Malik. In al-Tajnis: And by his view fatwa is given. And Ghayat al-Bayan followed him, and the explanation of when a judge rules its permissibility will come. In al-Khulasa: If one of two partners waqf his undivided share according to Abu Yusuf's view and then they divide, and the waqif's share falls in a place, he is not required to make it waqf again because division specifies the waqf property. And if he wants to avoid… the explanation of a consensual issue that ownership ceases by judicial decree, but if devoid of decree, it does not cease except after these conditions according to Muhammad, and the author chose this following the majority of mashayikh. Then he said: He sells and then makes the divided property waqf again. If the land was his and he waqf half of it then wants division, the way in that is… what remains then they divide. If he does not sell and raises it to the judge to order someone to divide with him, it is permissible; likewise in al-Khulasa. In it: A shop between two, one of them waqf his share and wants to affix the waqf plate on his door, the other partner prevents him; he may not affix unless the judge orders him, and this is Abu Yusuf's view; as for Muhammad's view, this cannot happen. In al-Zahiriyya: If he owned lands and houses in common with another, and waqf his share, then wants to partition with his partner and gather all the waqf in one land and one house, it is permissible according to the analogue of Abu Yusuf and Hilal. If the waqif partitions with his partner and there are dirhams between them, if the waqif is the one who gave the dirhams, it is permissible because in the waqf share he has divided with his partner and also purchased what was not waqf from his partner's share, so all that is permissible. Then the waqf share is for the waqif, and what he bought with the dirhams is his, not waqf. If he waqf all his land then part is claimed successfully, it becomes void in the rest according to Muhammad because the common ownership is concurrent, as in a gift, unlike if the donor retracts part, or the heir retracts two-thirds after the patient's death and he had given a gift or waqf while ill and the estate is constrained, because common ownership in that is supervening. If a specifically identified part is claimed successfully, it does not void the rest due to lack of common ownership, and thus it is permissible initially. And on this is gift and owned charity; as in al-Hidaya. If the land was between two men and they both waqf it for some purposes and deliver it to a guardian to manage it, that is permissible according to Muhammad because the preventer of completion of charity is common ownership in the subject of charity, and there is no common ownership here because all is charity. Except that, with many donors and the guardian taking possession of all at once, it is as if one man gave it as charity, unlike if each of them waqfd his half separately and appointed a separate guardian, that is not permissible because they are two charities. If each waqfd his share and they appointed one guardian and delivered both to him together, it is permissible because completion is by possession and possession can combine; as in Fath al-Qadir. Undivided property that is not partitioned: from sha'a yashi'u shay'aan wa shuyu'an wa musha'an; as in al-Qamus.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of waqf of an undivided share (musha') in property. The dispute is based on whether the delivery of possession (qabd) is a condition for the validity of waqf. Muhammad holds that delivery is necessary and that it requires separation (ifraz) of the waqf property from the owner's other property; thus waqf of an undivided share is invalid because separation is impossible while the share remains undivided. Abu Yusuf, on the other hand, does not require delivery as a condition for the validity of waqf; the waqf is complete upon the declaration, so waqf of an undivided share is permissible. The text explains that where division is impossible (e.g., a very small piece of land), both agree it is valid, except for mosques and cemeteries which are unanimously invalid as musha' due to the impossibility of exclusive dedication to Allah. The fatwa is stated to be on Muhammad's view, according to the authors of al-Khulasa, al-Bazaziyya, al-Walwalijiyya, and others. The passage then details various practical applications: if one partner waqfs his share on Abu Yusuf's view and then they partition, the waqf property is specified; if the waqif wants to affix a sign, he needs judicial permission on Abu Yusuf's view; combining waqfs from multiple donors into one administration is permissible if done collectively. It also discusses the effect of a successful claim (istihqaq) on part of the waqf property: if the part claimed is undifferentiated, the entire waqf is voided according to Muhammad (due to concurrent common ownership), but if it is a specific part, only that part is voided. The passage ends with a lexical definition of 'musha'.

Key Terms
المشاع (al-musha'): Undivided property; a share in common ownership that is not separated from the whole.
الإفراز (al-ifraz): The separation or partition of a share from the joint property, making it distinct.
القبض (al-qabd): Taking possession or delivery of the waqf property, considered by Muhammad as necessary for validity.

Checkpoint
Two partners each waqf their undivided shares in a land to a single guardian, delivering possession together. What is the ruling according to Muhammad?
A) Invalid, because each share is musha'.
B) Valid, because the entire land becomes waqf and possession is joint.
C) Valid only if they also sell the shares to each other first.
D) Invalid unless a judge approves.

Key Takeaway
In practical application, waqf of musha' requires careful navigation; collective waqf and judicial partition can achieve valid results, but the default ruling is invalidity per Muhammad.

The Dispute on Perpetuity (Ta'bid) in Waqf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on perpetuity condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the requirement of perpetuity in waqf and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) between them.

Core Idea
Waqf requires perpetuity, but Abu Yusuf allows temporary destinations if they eventually go to the poor.

This passage presents the third condition for a valid waqf: the destination (jihah) must be perpetual. The two imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) insist on a never-ending final destination; otherwise the waqf is invalid. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that waqf can have a terminating beneficiary class as long as the property ultimately goes to the poor. This disagreement stems from different views on the nature of waqf: is it primarily about removing ownership permanently, or about achieving divine proximity?

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed condition
The text examines whether the waqf's ultimate beneficiary class must be perpetual (never terminate). This is the third condition in the chapter.
The two say yes; Abu Yusuf says no.
2
State the position of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad)
They hold that waqf is invalid if the final destination is temporary. Their proof: waqf removes ownership without transferring it to anyone, similar to manumission (itq), which is perpetual. If the destination could end, the essential requirement (muqtada) of waqf is not fulfilled.
Analogy to itq: both involve removing ownership permanently.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his proof
Abu Yusuf argues that waqf is valid even with a temporary destination, because the underlying purpose is seeking nearness to Allah (taqarrub), which can be achieved through both temporary and permanent spending. Thus, waqf is valid in both cases.
He focuses on maqsad (objective) rather than mechanism.
4
Analyze the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Consider a person who makes a waqf for the maintenance of a mosque for 100 years with no further destination. According to the two, it is invalid because the destination terminates. According to Abu Yusuf, it is valid, and after 100 years it will go to the poor.
The difference is crucial when drafting waqf deeds.
5
Determine the operative position (mufta bihi)
The text notes that some say perpetuity is a condition by consensus. The word 'waqf' itself implies perpetuity. The majority view (the two) is the mufta bihi: waqf must have a perpetual final destination, but explicit mention of perpetuity is not required because the term 'waqf' indicates it.
Later Hanafi fatwa relies on the view of the two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث وهو أن يجعل آخره لجهة لا تنقطع فهو قولهما. وقال أبو يوسف : إذا فيه جهة تنقطع جاز وصار بعدها للفقراء، ولو لم يسمهم لهما أن موجب الوقف زوال الملك بدون التمليك وأنه يتأبد كالعتق وإذا كانت الجهة يتوهم انقطاعها لا يتوفر عليه مقتضاه ولهذا كان التوقيت مبطلاً له كالتوقيت في البيع. ولأبي يوسف أن المقصود هو التقرب إلى الله تعالى وهو موفر عليه لأن التقرب تارة يكون بالصرف إلى جهة تنقطع ومرة بالصرف إلى جهة تتأبد فصح في الوجهين . وقيل : التأبيد شرط بالإجماع إلا أن عند أبي يوسف لا يشترط ذكر التأبيد لأن لفظة الوقف والصدقة منبئة عنه لما بينا أنه إزالة الملك بدون التمليك كالعتق ولهذا قال في الكتاب في بيان قوله وصار بعدها للفقراء وإن لم يسمهم : سيأتي ذكر هذه المسألة في الورقة العشرين قوله : وصار) بعدها للفقراء ولو لم يسمهم) هذا مبني
As for the third [condition], that he makes its end a destination that does not terminate, that is the view of the two. Abu Yusuf said: If there is a destination that terminates, it is valid and it becomes after it for the poor, even if he did not name them. For the two, the requirement of waqf is the removal of ownership without making owned, and that it is perpetual like manumission. If the destination is likely to terminate, its requirement is not fulfilled, and therefore timing invalidates it like timing in a sale. For Abu Yusuf, the objective is drawing closer to Allah Almighty, and that is fulfilled because nearness sometimes is by spending on a terminating destination and sometimes by spending on an everlasting destination, so it is valid in both ways. It is said: Perpetuity is a condition by consensus, except that according to Abu Yusuf, mentioning perpetuity is not required because the word waqf and sadaqah indicate it, as we have said that it is removal of ownership without making owned like manumission. Hence he said in the book in explanation of his statement 'and it becomes after it for the poor even if he did not name them': This issue will be mentioned on page twenty. His statement: (and becomes) after it for the poor even if he did not name them, this is based on...
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents the third dispute in the chapter, concerning whether the waqf must be made perpetual (mu'abbad) by having a destination (jihah) that never ends. The view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that the final destination must be everlasting; if the waqf is made for a terminable purpose (e.g., for one's children and then no further purpose), it is invalid because waqf requires permanence like manumission (itq). Abu Yusuf, however, holds that the waqf is valid even if the named beneficiaries will eventually end, as long as after their extinction it goes to the poor. He argues that the aim of drawing near to Allah is achieved regardless of whether the destination is permanent or temporary. The text also notes that some say perpetuity is a condition by consensus, but Abu Yusuf does not require its explicit mention because the term 'waqf' itself implies perpetuity. The passage ends with a reference to a later explanation in the book (page twenty) regarding the ruling that it becomes for the poor even if not named. This shows the commentator's cross-referencing.

Key Terms
التأبيد (al-ta'bid): The quality of being perpetual; the requirement that the waqf's benefit continue indefinitely without termination.
الجهة المنقطعة (al-jihah al-munqati'ah): A destination (beneficiary class) that will eventually come to an end, e.g., specific descendants.
العتق (al-itq): Manumission of a slave; used as an analogy for the permanence of waqf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the view of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what is the ruling if someone makes a waqf for the benefit of his needy relatives and does not mention any destination after them?
A) The waqf is valid and after relatives it goes to the poor.
B) The waqf is invalid because the destination terminates.
C) The waqf is valid only if the relatives are poor.
D) The waqf is valid but must be renewed after relatives die.

Key Takeaway
Waqf requires perpetuity (ta'bid) in its final destination; Abu Yusuf's leniency does not override the majority view used for fatwa.

Ruling on Placing a Waqf Signboard on Common Property
Intra-Hanafi dispute on waqf signboard

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the waqf signboard dispute and apply the Hanafi principle that judicial authority can resolve co-ownership conflicts.

Core Idea
Waqf of a share does not grant rights over common property; a judge's order may enable signboard placement.

In the Hanafi school, when two people co-own a property and one waqfs his share, questions arise about using the common entrance. Can the waqif place a signboard indicating the waqf? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on this very issue, highlighting the tension between waqf rights and co-ownership.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Two partners own a shop. One partner waqfs his share. He wants to place a waqf signboard on the common door. The other partner objects. What are the legal options?
The key is that the property is jointly owned; the waqf pertains only to the waqif's share.
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Yusuf holds that the waqif cannot place the signboard unless the judge orders it. Muhammad holds that it cannot be done at all. Recognize that both are Hanafi imams.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab one.
3
Analyze the legal basis
Abu Yusuf relies on the judge's authority to resolve conflicts of use on common property. Muhammad argues that waqf of a share does not supersede the co-owner's right to prevent unilateral alterations.
The underlying principle: waqf does not grant ownership of the common area.
4
Determine the practical outcome
Under Abu Yusuf, the waqif can seek a court order. Under Muhammad, the signboard cannot be placed even with a judge's order. The thamarah al-khilaf is whether judicial intervention can override the co-owner's objection.
Later Hanafi works may prefer one view; here both are recorded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يبيع يقف المقسوم ثانياً، ولو كان الأرض له فوقف نصفها ثم أراد القسمة فالوجه في ذلك أن . ما بقي ثم يقتسمان، وإن لم يبع ورفع إلى القاضي ليأمر إنساناً بالقسمة معه جاز؛ كذا الخلاصة أيضاً. وفيها : حانوت بين اثنين وقف أحدهما نصيبه وأراد أن يضرب لوح الوقف على بابه فمنعه الشريك الآخر ليس له الضرب إلا إذا أمره القاضي بذلك وهذا قول أبي يوسف، أما على قول محمد فلا يتأتى هذا.
He sells, then the divided [property] stands as waqf a second time. If he owned land and waqfed half of it, then wanted division, the way in that is that... what remains, then they divide it. If he does not sell and raises it to the judge to order a person to divide with him, it is permissible. Likewise in al-Khulasa. In it: A shop between two persons, one of them waqfed his share and wanted to place the waqf signboard on his door, and the other partner prevented him. He does not have the right to place it unless the judge orders him to do so. This is the statement of Abu Yusuf. As for the statement of Muhammad, this does not occur.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi ruling on waqf and division. The author first states a general ruling: if a person waqfs half his land and then wants to divide with the other co-owner, the division is permissible after selling? The text has a lacuna. It then quotes al-Khulasa: for a shop owned by two, if one waqfs his share and wants to place a waqf sign on the common door, the other partner can prevent him unless the judge orders it. This is Abu Yusuf's view. Muhammad's view is that such a sign cannot be placed at all because the waqf partner cannot unilaterally act on common property. The key principle is that waqf does not automatically give the waqif the right to alter common property without judicial permission.

Key Terms
الوقف (al-waqf): Endowment: permanent dedication of property for charitable or religious purposes.
النصيب (al-nasīb): Share: undivided portion of jointly owned property.
القسمة (al-qisma): Division: partitioning of jointly owned property to determine individual shares.
لوح الوقف (lawḥ al-waqf): Waqf signboard: a physical sign indicating the property is waqf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, when can a partner who waqfed his share place a waqf signboard on a common door?
A) At any time without permission
B) Only if the other partner agrees
C) Only if the judge orders it
D) Never

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, waqf of a share does not give unilateral rights over common property; Abu Yusuf permits a signboard with a judge's order, while Muhammad forbids it.

Combining Waqf Shares through Division and Purchase
Ruling on combining waqf shares into one plot through division and purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on combining waqf shares through division and purchase, and identify the source of this ruling in the qiyas of Abu Yusuf and Hilal.

Core Idea
A waqif may consolidate waqf shares into one property and buy additional shares for himself without them becoming waqf.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from the Hanafi tradition: a person who has made waqf of his share in multiple properties co-owned with another. He wishes to divide the common property and consolidate his waqf interest into a single plot, and may even purchase his partner's share of the remaining property. The passage, cited from al-Zahiriya, declares this permissible based on the analogical reasoning (qiyas) of Abu Yusuf and Hilal. Understanding this ruling clarifies the flexibility of waqf management within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is consolidating waqf shares into a single property through division with a co-owner and optionally buying the co-owner's non-waqf share. The ruling is permissible (ja'iz).
The permissibility is based on qiyas, not a direct scriptural text.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is qiyas al-qawl (analogical extension of an opinion) of Abu Yusuf and Hilal, as cited from the text al-Zahiriya.
This is not from the primary sources (Zahir al-Riwayah) but from the nawadir.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The qiyas analogizes the situation of dividing common property to the permissibility of a waqif purchasing property for himself; the essence is that the waqf remains intact and the personal property can be bought separately.
The exact analogy is not expanded in the text but implied by the context.
4
Note the authority level
This ruling is from al-Zahiriya, a later Hanafi text, and is attributed to Abu Yusuf and Hilal. It is not from the canonical Zahir al-Riwayah, so it carries less authority.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is not specified; a mufti would check if this is accepted.
5
Evaluate the practical effect
The ruling allows flexibility in managing waqf property: a waqif can consolidate scattered waqf shares without losing the waqf character, and can buy additional property for himself.
The bought property does not become waqf unless intended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية ولو كانت له أرضون ودور بينه وبين آخر فوقف نصيبه ثم أراد أن يقاسم شريكه ويجمع الوقف كله في أرض واحدة ودار واحدة فإنه جائز في قياس قول أبي يوسف وهلال، وإذا قاسم الواقف شريكه وبينهما دراهم فإن كان الواقف هو الذي أعطى الدراهم جاز لأنه في حصة الوقف قاسم شریکه واشترى أيضاً ما لم يقف من نصيب شريكه فجاز ذلك كله، ثم حصة الوقف للواقف وما اشتراه بالدراهم فذلك له وليس بوقف اهـ.
And in al-Zahiriya: If he had lands and houses between him and another, and he waqfed his share, then wanted to divide with his partner and gather all the waqf in one land and one house, then it is permissible in the qiyas of the statement of Abu Yusuf and Hilal. And if the waqif divides with his partner and there are dirhams between them, and if the waqif is the one who gave the dirhams, it is permissible, because he divided with his partner concerning the waqf share and also bought what was not waqf from his partner's share, so all of that is permissible. Then the waqf share is for the waqif, and what he bought with the dirhams is for him, not waqf. End.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage cites al-Zahiriya on a specific scenario: a person who owns multiple properties in common with another, and he waqfs his share in all of them. He then wants to divide the common property and consolidate his waqf share into a single land and a single house. According to the qiyas of Abu Yusuf and Hilal, this is permissible. If during the division the waqif pays his partner some dirhams to buy the partner's share of the non-waqf portion, that purchase is valid and the purchased property becomes the waqif's personal property, not waqf. The ruling shows that waqf shares can be relocated through division and purchase, as long as the waqf's essence remains intact.

Key Terms
الظهيرية (al-Zahiriya): A Hanafi fiqh text by al-Zahir al-Marghimani? Actually a reference work.
قياس القول (qiyās al-qawl): Analogical extension of a legal opinion (not scriptural text).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a waqif buys his partner's non-waqf share during division of common property, what is the status of that bought property?
A) It becomes part of the waqf
B) It remains the partner's property
C) It becomes the waqif's personal property
D) It must be donated to charity

Key Takeaway
A waqif can consolidate waqf shares through division and may purchase additional shares for himself without them becoming waqf.

Ruling on Partial Istihqaq of Waqf Property
Ruling on partial istihqaq of waqf property with distinction based on type of share.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on partial istihqaq of waqf property and distinguish between original and accidental shuyu'.

Core Idea
If an undivided share of waqf is reclaimed, the whole voids; if a specific part is claimed, the rest remains valid.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (quoting Al-Hidayah) addresses a subtle but critical distinction in waqf law: what happens when a third party successfully claims ownership of part of a waqf property? According to Imam Muhammad, the answer depends on whether the claim is for an undivided share (creating original shuyu') or for a specific, distinct part. The ruling is derived by analogy to gift (hiba) and hinges on the type of commingling involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is waqf (endowment) of all one's land, followed by istihqaq (ownership claim) of a part. The ruling: the waqf becomes void in the remaining part if the claim is for an undivided share; but if the claim is for a specific distinct part, the rest remains valid.
Ruling attributed to Imam Muhammad.
2
Understand the key distinction
The distinction is between original shuyu' (shuyu' muqarin) and accidental shuyu' (shuyu' tari'). Original shuyu' exists from the start, making the waqf defective; accidental shuyu' arises later and does not void the whole.
Shuyu' means undivided co-ownership.
3
Apply the analogy to gift (hiba)
The ruling is analogized to gift: if a donor gives a gift and then takes back part, the whole gift becomes void because the gift was based on taking possession (qabd) which cannot be partial. Similarly, waqf requires transfer of possession, and original shuyu' prevents that.
Contrast with the case where the donor or heir revokes part after death due to debt restrictions.
4
Recognize the exception for a specific distinct part
If the claim is for a specific, delineated parcel (e.g., one field out of many), there is no shuyu' affecting the rest. Hence, only that part is removed and the remaining waqf remains valid.
This is consistent with the initial validity of a waqf of specific parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وقف جميع أرضه ثم استحق جزء منه بطل في الباقي عند محمد لأن الشيوع مقارن كما في الهبة بخلاف ما إذا رجع الواهب في البعض أو رجع الوارث في الثلثين بعد موت المريض وقد وهب أو وقف في مرضه وفي المال ضيق لأن الشيوع في ذلك طار، ولو استحق جزء مميز بعينه لم يبطل في الباقي لعدم الشيوع ولهذا جاز في الابتداء، وعلى هذا الهبة والصدقة المملوكة ؛ كذا في الهداية
And if he waqfed all his land, then part of it is claimed (istihaqq), then the waqf becomes void in the remaining part according to Muhammad, because the commingling (shuyu') is original (muqarin), as in gift (hiba). Unlike the case when the donor takes back part, or the heir takes back two-thirds after the patient's death when he had gifted or waqfed in his illness while the wealth was straitened, because the commingling in that is accidental (tari'). And if a specific distinct part is claimed, it does not void the remaining, due to absence of commingling; for this reason, it was valid initially. And upon this is gift and owned charity. As in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of a third party successfully claiming (istihqaq) part of waqf property. According to Imam Muhammad, if the claim is for an undivided share (e.g., 1/3 of the land), the entire waqf becomes void in the remaining part because the original dedication was subject to an inherent shuyu' (commingling) that makes the waqf defective. This is analogous to a gift where the donor takes back part, voiding the whole. However, if the claim is for a specific, distinct parcel (e.g., a particular field), then only that part is removed and the waqf remains valid for the rest. The distinction hinges on whether the shuyu' is original (muqarin) or accidental (tari'). The same ruling applies to gifts and sadaqa.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istihqāq): Legal claim of ownership by a third party that removes property from possession.
شيوع مقارن (shuyū' muqārin): Original commingling: undivided share inherent from the start of the contract.
شيوع طار (shuyū' ṭāri'): Accidental commingling: arises later, e.g., from revocation by heir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the istihqaq of part of waqf land not void the remaining waqf according to Muhammad?
A) When the claim is for an undivided share
B) When the claim is for a specific, distinct part
C) When the waqf was made in illness
D) When the claimant is the waqif himself

Key Takeaway
Partial istihqaq of waqf voids the whole only if the claim is for an undivided share (original shuyu'); a specific part claim leaves the rest valid.

Ruling on Joint Waqf by Partners and the Concept of Musha'
Ruling on two partners waqfiing their land together and lexical definition of musha'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on joint waqf by two partners, explain the role of shuyu' as an impediment, and distinguish the three scenarios presented.

Core Idea
Joint waqf by two partners to a single administrator is valid because the whole becomes charity, eliminating shuyu'.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses a scenario where two partners wish to waqf their jointly owned land. The ruling distinguishes between waqfiing the entire property together versus waqfiing separate shares. Understanding the concept of shuyu' (undivided shares) is key to applying the correct ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (joint waqf by two partners) and the overall ruling (valid under certain conditions, invalid under others).
The ruling comes from Imam Muhammad within the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the rationale (talil)
The text states that the impediment to a valid charity is shuyu' (commingling) in the subject of charity. Here, because the entire land is dedicated as one waqf, there is no shuyu'.
This reasoning is the 'proof' (dalil) for the ruling.
3
Distinguish the three scenarios
Identify the three cases: (1) joint waqf to one administrator – valid; (2) separate waqfs with separate administrators – invalid; (3) separate shares but same administrator and collective possession – valid.
The key factor is whether the possession (qabd) is combined and the waqf is treated as one.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Recognize that this is the view of Muhammad; other Hanafi scholars may have differing positions on joint waqf.
The text does not specify the opposing view, but it is acknowledged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانت الأرض بين رجلين فوقفاها على بعض الوجوه ودفعاها إلى وال يقوم عليها كان ذلك جائزاً عند محمد لأن المانع من تمام الصدقة شيوع في المحل المتصدق به ولا شيوع هنا لأن الكل صدقة. غاية الأمر أن ذلك مع كثرة المتصدقين والقبض من الوالي في الكل وجد جملة واحدة فهو كما لو تصدق بها رجل واحد بخلاف ما لو وقف كل منهما نصفها شائعاً على حدة وجعل لها والياً على حدة لا يجوز لأنهما صدقتان، ولو وقف كل منهما نصيبه وجعلا الوالي فسلمها إليه جميعاً جاز لأن تمامها بالقبض والقبض يجتمع ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. والمشاع غير المقسوم من شاع يشيع شيعاً وشيوعاً ومشاعاً؛ كذا في القاموس.
And if the land was between two men, and they waqfed it for some purpose and handed it over to a guardian to manage it, that is permissible according to Muhammad, because the impediment to the completion of charity is shuyu' in the subject of charity, and there is no shuyu' here because the whole is charity. The point is that despite the multiplicity of donors, the possession by the guardian of the whole occurs as one single act, so it is as if one man gave charity. Unlike if each of them waqfed his half separately and appointed a separate guardian, then it is not permissible because they are two charities. And if each waqfed his share and they appointed one guardian and handed it over to him together, it is permissible because its completion depends on possession, and possession is combined. As in Fath al-Qadir. And 'musha' (undivided) is from sha'a yashi'u shai'an wa shuyu'an wa musha'an. As in al-Qamus.
Source pages p. 330

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Muhammad: if two partners jointly waqf the entire land to a single administrator, it is valid because the entire property becomes sadaqa (charity) with no shuyu'. The delivery of possession to the administrator is done collectively, as if one person gave charity. However, if each partner waqfs his half separately with separate administrators, it is invalid because they are two distinct charities with shuyu'. If they waqf their shares individually but appoint the same administrator and hand over together, it is valid because possession is combined (ijtima' al-qabd). The passage ends with a lexical definition of 'musha'' (undivided share) from al-Qamus.

Key Terms
المشاع (al-mushā'): Undivided share in jointly owned property; commingled.
القبض (al-qabd): Possession; physical or legal delivery of the waqf property to the administrator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the impediment that makes separate waqfs by partners on undivided shares invalid?
A) Lack of intention
B) Shuyu' (commingling) in the subject of charity
C) Absence of a guardian
D) Multiplicity of donors

Key Takeaway
Joint waqf by partners to a single administrator is valid because the entire property becomes one charity, avoiding the impediment of shuyu'.

Two Narrations from Abu Yusuf on Ta'bid in Waqf
Two narrations from Abu Yusuf on ta'bid and the distinction between waqf and sadaqa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two narrations from Abu Yusuf regarding ta'bid in waqf, explain the distinction between explicit mention and the meaning of perpetuity, and identify the fatwa position.

Core Idea
The dispute on ta'bid is about mentioning perpetuity, not its necessity — it is a condition by consensus.

Earlier, you learned about the requirement of ta'bid (perpetuity) in waqf and a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his students. Now we refine that: within Abu Yusuf's own positions, there are two narrations. This lesson unpacks them, showing that the real point of contention is whether perpetuity must be explicitly stated or can be implied. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that if the word 'sadaqa' is used, the two narrations apply; but if only 'waqf' is used, the ruling changes. A critical refinement reveals that all agree on ta'bid in meaning; the disagreement is only about its explicit expression.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrations from Abu Yusuf
Read the opening: 'wa al-hasil 'an Abi Yusuf fi al-ta'bid riwayatayn: fi riwaya la budda minhu wa dhikruhu laysa bi shart...' State each narration clearly: (1) ta'bid is necessary but its mention is not a condition; (2) ta'bid is not a condition at all.
The second narration says ta'bid is not a condition—meaning the waqf can be valid without perpetual intention.
2
Apply the narrations to a concrete case
Consider a waqf on a specific person (say 'Zaid') without mentioning perpetuity. If Zaid dies, what happens? Under narration 1, the property returns to the waqif's heirs. Under narration 2, it goes to the poor.
Al-Natif prefers the first narration as the fatwa.
3
Distinguish between using 'sadaqa' and 'waqf'
The passage notes that the two narrations apply only when the word 'sadaqa' is used. If the word 'waqf' alone is used with a specific beneficiary, it is invalid by agreement (according to the second narration).
The custom ('urf) interprets absolute 'waqf' as for the poor, restricting it to a specific person breaks that custom.
4
Understand the refinement: ta'bid in meaning is a condition by consensus
The text from al-Is'af clarifies that the khilaf is only about explicit mention (al-tansis 'alayhi) or its substitute (like 'poor'). The meaning of perpetuity (ta'bid ma'nan) is a condition by agreement of all scholars.
This resolves the apparent earlier contradiction: the dispute is not about the necessity of perpetuity itself.
5
Determine the fatwa and its authority
Al-Natif says the first narration (return to heirs) is the fatwa, but al-Ramli considers the second narration weak. Recognize that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with different weights of riwaya.
The fatwa hierarchy: al-Natif's view > al-Hidayah's correction > al-Ramli's critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل عن أبي يوسف في التأبيد روايتين : في رواية لا بد منه وذكره ليس بشرط وصححه، وفي رواية ليس بشرط . وتفرع على الروايتين ما لو وقف على إنسان بعينه أو عليه وعلى أولاده أو على قرابته وهم يحصون أو على أمهات أولاده فمات الموقوف عليه، فعلى الأول يعود إلى ورثة الواقف . قال الناطفي في الأجناس وعليه الفتوى وعلى الثاني تصرف إلى الفقراء وهي رواية البرامكة ؛ كذا في فتح القدير وظاهر ما في المجتبى والخلاصة أن الروايتين عنه فيما إذا ذكر لفظ الصدقة، أما إذا ذكر لفظ الوقف فقط فلا يجوز اتفاقاً إذا كان الموقوف عليه معيناً على الرواية الثانية عن أبي يوسف كما يأتي كما نبه عليه في الفتح قوله : (قال الناطفي في الأجناس وعليه الفتوى مخالف لما صححه في الهداية كما تقدم آنفاً لكن قال الرملي : ارجع إلى النهر فإنه ذكر أنه رواية ضعيفة عنه أي عن أبي يوسف اهـ قلت: وفي الإسعاف ولو قال وقفت أرضي هذه على ولد زيد وذكر جماعة بأعيانهم لم يصح عند أبي يوسف أيضاً لأن تعين الموقوف عليه يمنع إرادة غيره بخلاف ما إذا لم يعين لجعله إياه على الفقراء ألا ترى أنه فرق بين قوله أرضي هذه موقوفة» وبين قوله «موقوفة على ولدي فصحح الأول دون الثاني لأن مطلق قوله «موقوفة» يصرف إلى الفقراء عرفاً فإذا ذكر الولد صار مقيداً فلا يبقى العرف فظهر بهذا أن الخلاف بينهما في اشتراط ذكر التأبيد، وعدمه إنما هو في التنصيص عليه أو على ما يقوم مقامه كالفقراء ونحوهم ، وأما التأبيد معنى فشرط اتفاقاً على الصحيح وقد نص عليه محققو المشايخ اهـ ما في الإسعاف.
And the summary from Abu Yusuf regarding perpetuity (ta'bid) is two narrations: in one narration, it is necessary and its mention is not a condition, and he corrected it; in another narration, it is not a condition. And branches from the two narrations: if he waqfed on a specific person, or on him and his children, or on his relatives who are countable, or on the mothers of his children, and the beneficiary dies, then according to the first (narration) it returns to the heirs of the waqif. Al-Natif said in al-Ajnas: and upon it is the fatwa. According to the second, it is dispensed to the poor, and that is the narration of al-Baramika. As in Fath al-Qadir. And apparent from al-Mujtaba and al-Khulasa is that the two narrations from him are when he mentioned the word sadaqa. But if he mentioned only the word waqf, then it is invalid by agreement when the beneficiary is specific, according to the second narration from Abu Yusuf, as will come, as pointed out in al-Fath. He said: (al-Natif said in al-Ajnas: and upon it is the fatwa, which contradicts what was corrected in al-Hidayah as mentioned earlier, but al-Ramli said: refer to al-Nahr, for it mentioned that it is a weak narration from him, i.e., from Abu Yusuf). End. I said: In al-Is'af: if he said 'I waqfed this land of mine on the son of Zaid' and mentioned a group of individuals, it is invalid according to Abu Yusuf as well, because specifying the beneficiary prevents intending others, unlike when he does not specify, as he makes it for the poor. Do you not see that he differentiated between saying 'my land is waqf' and 'waqf on my son': he validated the former without the latter, because the absolute 'waqf' is understood by custom to be for the poor. When he mentioned the son, it becomes restricted, so the custom does not remain. Thus it appears that the disagreement between them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the condition of mentioning perpetuity is only about explicitly stating it or what takes its place like 'the poor' and the like. As for the meaning of perpetuity, it is a condition by consensus correctly, and the verifying scholars have stated this. End of what is in al-Is'af.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage refines the earlier dispute on ta'bid by presenting two narrations from Abu Yusuf. According to one narration, perpetuity is necessary but its explicit mention is not a condition; according to the other, it is not a condition at all. The practical consequence: if a waqf is made on specified individuals (e.g., a person, his children, etc.) and they all die, under the first narration the property returns to the waqif's heirs; under the second, it goes to the poor. Al-Natif says the first is the fatwa. However, the passage then notes that these narrations only apply when the word 'sadaqa' is used. If only the word 'waqf' is used with a specific beneficiary, it is invalid by agreement. The text from al-Is'af clarifies that the real issue is whether the wording implies perpetuity, not whether perpetuity is a condition — it is a condition by consensus. The dispute is only about explicit mention.

Key Terms
الرواية (al-riwāya): Narration or transmitted version of a legal opinion from a Hanafi imam.
الفتوى (al-fatwā): The relied-upon legal ruling issued for practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition about ta'bid that all scholars agree upon?
A) Explicit mention of ta'bid is required.
B) Ta'bid in meaning is a condition by consensus.
C) Ta'bid is never a condition for waqf.
D) Only the second narration from Abu Yusuf is correct.

Key Takeaway
Ta'bid in meaning is a condition for waqf by consensus; the intra-Hanafi dispute is limited to whether it must be explicitly stated.

Requirement of Ta'bid in Waqf and Abu Yusuf's Two Narrations
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ta'bid requirement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi condition of perpetuity (ta'bid) in waqf, distinguish the two narrations of Abu Yusuf, and apply the mufta bihi ruling to a case of specified beneficiaries.

Core Idea
A specified-beneficiary waqf without explicit perpetuity may return to heirs upon extinction.

Waqf is a perpetual charity in Hanafi fiqh, but what if the waqif only says 'I give this as sadaqah' to a specific person? Does the waqf end when that person dies? This lesson explores a key intra-Hanafi dispute about the need to explicitly mention perpetuity, and the practical consequences of Abu Yusuf's two narrations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core requirement
Understand that according to Muhammad, explicit mention of ta'bid is a condition because waqf as sadaqah of produce can be temporary.
The source text: 'because this is sadaqah of benefit and produce, and that may be temporary'.
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's two narrations
One narration requires ta'bid but not its mention; the other does not require it at all.
These narrations are attributed to Abu Yusuf alone; Muhammad disagrees.
3
Trace the thamarah al-khilaf
If the beneficiary dies, on the first narration the waqf returns to the waqif's heirs; on the second, it goes to the poor.
This practical difference is the key consequence.
4
Apply the tarjih
The fatwa is on the first narration (return to heirs) according to al-Natifi in al-Ajnas.
This is the mufta bihi for fatwa.
5
Understand the qualification
The two narrations apply only when the word 'sadaqah' is used; if only 'waqf' is stated and the beneficiary is specified, it is invalid by consensus.
This restricts the scope of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا هو الصحيح. وعند محمد ذكر التأبيد شرط لأن هذا صدقة بالمنفعة وبالغلة وذلك قد يكون موقتاً فمطلقه لا ينصرف إلى التأبيد فلا بد من التنصيص ؛ كذا في الهداية. والحاصل عن أبي يوسف في التأبيد روايتين : في رواية لا بد منه وذكره ليس بشرط وصححه، وفي رواية ليس بشرط . وتفرع على الروايتين ما لو وقف على إنسان بعينه أو عليه وعلى أولاده أو على قرابته وهم يحصون أو على أمهات أولاده فمات الموقوف عليه، فعلى الأول يعود إلى ورثة الواقف . قال الناطفي في الأجناس وعليه الفتوى وعلى الثاني تصرف إلى الفقراء وهي رواية البرامكة ؛ كذا في فتح القدير وظاهر ما في المجتبى والخلاصة أن الروايتين عنه فيما إذا ذكر لفظ الصدقة، أما إذا ذكر لفظ الوقف فقط فلا يجوز اتفاقاً إذا كان الموقوف عليه معيناً
And this is correct. According to Muhammad, mentioning perpetuity is a condition because this is sadaqah of benefit and produce, and that may be temporary, so its absolute form does not extend to perpetuity, so specification is necessary; as in al-Hidayah. The summary from Abu Yusuf regarding perpetuity is two narrations: in one narration it is necessary and its mention is not a condition, and he corrected it; in another narration it is not a condition. And based on the two narrations, if one makes a waqf on a specific person, or on him and his children, or on his relatives who are countable, or on his ummahat awlad, and the beneficiary dies, then on the first [narration] it returns to the heirs of the waqif. al-Natifi said in al-Ajnas: and upon it is the fatwa. On the second, it is given to the poor, and it is the narration of al-Baramika; as in Fath al-Qadir. The apparent from al-Mujtaba and al-Khulasa is that the two narrations from him are when he mentions the word sadaqah; if he only mentions the word waqf, then it is not valid by agreement if the beneficiary is specified.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the condition of perpetuity (ta'bid) in waqf. The author begins by affirming the correct position that perpetuity is required. According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, explicitly mentioning perpetuity is a condition because waqf is a sadaqah involving benefit and produce, which can be temporary; therefore, the absolute word 'waqf' does not imply perpetuity without specification. From Abu Yusuf, there are two narrations: one requiring mention of perpetuity (though he deemed its mention not a condition, perhaps meaning the word itself is not needed), and another where it is not a condition at all. The practical consequence (thamar) of these narrations is illustrated: if a waqf is made on a specific person or limited group who later die, on the first narration the property returns to the waqif's heirs (as the waqf is extinguished), and on the second narration it goes to the poor (as the waqf continues for general charity). The fatwa, according to al-Natifi, is on the first narration. However, a further qualification is added: this ikhtilaf applies only when the word 'sadaqah' is mentioned; if only 'waqf' is stated and the beneficiary is specified, it is invalid by agreement.

Key Terms
التأبيد (al-ta'bid): Perpetuity; the condition that a waqf must be perpetual and not temporary.
الموقوف عليه (al-mawquf 'alayh): The beneficiary of a waqf; the person or group entitled to receive its usufruct or produce.
الروايتان (al-riwayatan): Two narrations attributed to Abu Yusuf regarding the condition of ta'bid in waqf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in this passage, what happens to a waqf made on a specific person using the word 'sadaqah' when that person dies?
A) The waqf continues for the poor.
B) The waqf returns to the waqif's heirs.
C) The waqf becomes null and void from the beginning.
D) The waqf is divided between the poor and the heirs.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf, perpetuity is required; if a waqf on specified beneficiaries uses the word 'sadaqah', the mufta bihi is that upon their death the property returns to the waqif's heirs.

The Principle of Need and Perpetuity in Waqf
Explanation of the rule that need implies perpetuity, with contrast to invalid public utility waqfs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that mentioning a 'place of need' suffices for perpetuity in waqf and distinguish valid from invalid public utility waqfs.

Core Idea
Mentioning need makes waqf perpetual; public utilities other than mosques are invalid due to lack of custom.

This lesson explores the foundational Hanafi principle that a waqf must be perpetual (mu'abbad). The passage from Bahr al-Raiq explains that when the waqif mentions a category of need (e.g., the poor), that itself indicates perpetuity, without needing explicit words. However, waqf for public infrastructure like bridges or roads is not automatically valid, unlike mosques, because custom ('urf) supports mosques but not other utilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the beneficiary description
Recognize that mentioning a 'place of need' (mawdi' al-hajah) in a perpetual manner suffices for validity without additional wording like 'sadaqah'.
Examples given: wayfarers and the chronically ill.
2
Apply the rule to specific cases
For wayfarers and the chronically ill, the waqf is valid and the produce goes to the poor among them, because need is ongoing.
Need implies perpetuity.
3
Compare with public utility waqfs
Abu Hanifa rules that waqf for building bridges, repairing roads, digging graves, making water stations, or purchasing shrouds for poor Muslims is invalid.
The contrast is with mosques, which are valid due to custom.
4
Understand the role of custom
Custom ('urf) is the basis for treating mosques as perpetual; other public utilities lack this customary perpetuity and are therefore invalid.
The ruling is specific to Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : متى ذكر موضع الحاجة على وجه يتأبد يكفيه عن ذكر الصدقة، وكذا على أبناء السبيل أو الزمني ويكون للفقراء منهم وفي الخلاصة والبزازية قال أبو حنيفة : إذا وقف مالاً لبناء القناطر أو لإصلاح الطريق أو لحفر القبور أو لاتخاذ السقايات أو لشراء الأكفان لفقراء المسلمين لا يجوز بخلاف الوقف للمساجد الجريان العادة بالثاني دون الأول. وقف على فقراء مكة أو فقراء قرية معروفة إن كانوا لا يحصون يجوز في الحياة وبعد الممات لأنه مؤبد، وإن كانوا يحصون يجوز بعد الموت لأنه وصية والوصية لقوم يحصون تجوز حتى إذا انقرضوا صار ميراثاً منهم، وإن كان في الحياة لا يجوز وقف أرضه على عمارة مصاحف موقوفة لا يصح لأنه لا عرف فيه. وقف على أمهات أولاده وعبيده فالوقف باطل في قول هلال. وفي الفتاوى: وقف على أمهات أولاده إلا من تزوج فلا شيء لها، فإن طلقها زوجها لا يعود حقها الساقط إلا إذا كان الواقف استثنى وقال من طلقت فلها أيضاً قسط من الوقف. وذكر الخصاف : قال أرضي هذه صدقة موقوفة الله تعالى على الناس أو على بني آدم أو على أهل بغداد أبداً فإذا انقرضوا فعلى المساكين أو على العميان أو على الزمني فالوقف باطل. وذكر في موضع آخر مسألة العميان والزمني وقال : الغلة للمساكين لا لهما. ولو وقف على قراء القرآن والفقراء فالوقف باطل. وذكر هلال الوقف على الزمني المنقطعين صحيح . وقال المشايخ : الوقف على معلم المسجد الذي يعلم الصبيان غير صحيح. وقيل: يصح لأن الفقر غالب فيهم. قال شمس الأئمة فعلى هذا إذا وقف على طلبة علم بلدة كذا يجوز لأن الفقر غالب فيهم فكان الاسم منبئاً عن الحاجة والحاصل أنه متى ذكر مصرفاً فيه نص على الفقراء والحاجة فالوقف صحيح يحصون أم لا. وقوله يحصون» إشارة إلى أن التأبيد ليس بشرط ومتى ذكر مصرفاً يستوي فيه الغني والفقير إن كانوا يحصون صح بطريق التمليك، وإن كانوا لا يحصون فهو باطل إلا أن يكون في لفظه ما يدل على الحاجة كاليتامي فحينئذ إن كانوا يحصون فالأغنياء والفقراء سواء، وإن لا يحصون فالوقف صحيح ويصرف إلى فقرائهم لا إلى أغنيائهم، وكذا لو وقف على الزمني فهو على فقرائهم. وفي الفتاوي : لو وقف على الخصاف
Then he said: Whenever he mentions a place of need in a way that is perpetual, it suffices him from mentioning sadaqah. Similarly, [if he makes a waqf] for wayfarers or the chronically ill, then it is for the poor among them. In al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya, Abu Hanifa said: If he endows wealth for building bridges, or for repairing roads, or for digging graves, or for making water stations, or for purchasing shrouds for poor Muslims, it is not valid, unlike the waqf for mosques due to customary practice of the second but not the first. A waqf on the poor of Makkah or the poor of a known village: if they are not countable, it is valid during life and after death because it is perpetual; if they are countable, it is valid after death because it is a will, and a will for a countable group is valid, and when they become extinct it becomes inheritance from them; but during life it is not valid. A waqf of his land for the maintenance of endowed copies of the Quran is not valid because there is no custom for it. A waqf on his ummahat awlad and his slaves: the waqf is void according to Hilal. In al-Fatawa: A waqf on his ummahat awlad except that one who marries has no share; if her husband divorces her, her fallen right does not return unless the waqif made an exception and said 'whoever is divorced also has a portion of the waqf.' Al-Khassaf mentioned: He said, 'This land of mine is sadaqah, endowed to Allah, upon the people, or upon the sons of Adam, or upon the people of Baghdad forever, and when they become extinct then upon the poor, or upon the blind, or upon the chronically ill' — the waqf is void. He mentioned in another place the issue of the blind and the chronically ill, and said: The produce is for the poor, not for them. If one makes a waqf on reciters of the Quran and the poor, the waqf is void. Hilal mentioned that a waqf on the chronically ill who are confined is valid. The mashayikh said: A waqf on the teacher of the mosque who teaches children is not valid. It was said: It is valid because poverty is prevalent among them. Shams al-A'imma said: On this basis, if one makes a waqf on students of a certain town, it is valid because poverty is prevalent among them, so the name indicates need. The summary: Whenever he mentions a channel of disbursement that contains a text regarding the poor and need, the waqf is valid whether they are countable or not. His saying 'countable' is an indication that perpetuity is not a condition. And whenever he mentions a channel of disbursement that includes both rich and poor: if they are countable, it is valid by way of ownership; if they are not countable, it is void unless there is in his wording what indicates need, like 'orphans' — then if they are countable, the rich and poor are equal; if they are not countable, the waqf is valid and is paid to their poor, not to their rich. Similarly, if he makes a waqf on the chronically ill, it is for their poor. In al-Fatawa: If he makes a waqf on al-Khassaf...
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings (furu') regarding the validity of waqf based on the nature of the beneficiary or purpose. The key principle is that waqf must be perpetual; therefore, beneficiaries must be either unlimited in number or inherently poor (since poverty is an enduring condition). The text begins by stating that mentioning a 'place of need' (e.g., the poor) suffices for perpetuity without explicit ta'bid. For example, waqf for wayfarers or the chronically ill is valid, with the produce going to the poor among them. Abu Hanifa rules that waqf for public utilities like bridges, roads, graves, water stations, and shrouds is invalid, unlike mosques, due to custom ('urf). Waqf on the poor of a city is valid if they are not countable, as it is perpetual; if countable, it is treated as a will (valid only after death). Waqf on copies of the Quran is invalid due to lack of custom. Waqf on one's ummahat awlad and slaves is void according to Hilal, with a conditional exception in al-Fatawa. al-Khassaf discusses cases where the beneficiary is too broad (e.g., all people) or includes the blind/chronically ill without specifying poverty; such waqfs are void. However, if the beneficiary is described in a way that indicates need (like orphans or students where poverty is prevalent), the waqf is valid and goes to the poor among them. The concluding summary distinguishes between waqf for a class that includes both rich and poor: if countable, it is a valid ownership-based waqf but if not countable, it is void unless there is an indication of need (like 'orphans'), in which case it goes to the poor only.

Key Terms
موضع الحاجة (mawdi' al-hajah): A place of need; a category of beneficiaries that are in need, like the poor, orphans, etc.
الزمني (al-zamana): The chronically ill or disabled; considered a category of need.
أبناء السبيل (abna' al-sabil): Wayfarers; travelers who are in need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because bridges are not mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because beneficiaries are countable.
C) Because there is no custom ('urf) for it.
D) Because it is not perpetual.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf, mentioning a need category implies perpetuity; public utilities other than mosques are invalid due to lack of custom.

Waqf on Countable Groups and Need Indicators
Detailed cases of waqf validity based on countability and need indicators, with summary principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the validity of a waqf based on whether the beneficiaries are countable and whether the wording indicates need, and apply the rule that waqf for uncountable groups with need indication is valid for the poor only.

Core Idea
Countable beneficiaries reduce waqf to a will; need indication (like orphans) restores perpetuity for the poor only.

This lesson continues the exploration of waqf validity by focusing on groups with countable members. The passage explains that if beneficiaries are countable (like the poor of a small village), the waqf is not perpetual and is treated as a will, valid only after death. However, if the wording indicates need—such as 'orphans' or 'students' where poverty is prevalent—the waqf is valid and goes to the poor among them. The summary ties together these conditions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish countable vs uncountable beneficiaries
Determine if the beneficiary group can be counted (yuhsawn). If yes, the waqf lacks inherent perpetuity and is instead treated as a will (valid after death only).
Example: poor of a known small village.
2
Check for need indication in the wording
If the wording includes a term indicating need (e.g., orphans, poor, students), then even if countable, the waqf is valid for the poor among them.
Need indication overrides the countability issue.
3
Apply to specific categories
For the blind, chronically ill, or students, if the term indicates need (e.g., 'blind' alone does not, but 'orphans' does), the waqf goes to the poor.
The passage illustrates with 'zamanah' and 'talaba'.
4
Understand the condition of poverty indication
If the beneficiary includes both rich and poor without need wording, the waqf is invalid unless the group is countable (then it is a valid ownership-based waqf like a will).
The summary clarifies this.
5
Identify exceptions and disputes
The passage discusses disagreement on waqf for teachers and students: some deem invalid, but Shams al-A'imma allows if poverty is prevalent.
The ikhtilaf is resolved by tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : متى ذكر موضع الحاجة على وجه يتأبد يكفيه عن ذكر الصدقة، وكذا على أبناء السبيل أو الزمني ويكون للفقراء منهم وفي الخلاصة والبزازية قال أبو حنيفة : إذا وقف مالاً لبناء القناطر أو لإصلاح الطريق أو لحفر القبور أو لاتخاذ السقايات أو لشراء الأكفان لفقراء المسلمين لا يجوز بخلاف الوقف للمساجد الجريان العادة بالثاني دون الأول. وقف على فقراء مكة أو فقراء قرية معروفة إن كانوا لا يحصون يجوز في الحياة وبعد الممات لأنه مؤبد، وإن كانوا يحصون يجوز بعد الموت لأنه وصية والوصية لقوم يحصون تجوز حتى إذا انقرضوا صار ميراثاً منهم، وإن كان في الحياة لا يجوز وقف أرضه على عمارة مصاحف موقوفة لا يصح لأنه لا عرف فيه. وقف على أمهات أولاده وعبيده فالوقف باطل في قول هلال. وفي الفتاوى: وقف على أمهات أولاده إلا من تزوج فلا شيء لها، فإن طلقها زوجها لا يعود حقها الساقط إلا إذا كان الواقف استثنى وقال من طلقت فلها أيضاً قسط من الوقف. وذكر الخصاف : قال أرضي هذه صدقة موقوفة الله تعالى على الناس أو على بني آدم أو على أهل بغداد أبداً فإذا انقرضوا فعلى المساكين أو على العميان أو على الزمني فالوقف باطل. وذكر في موضع آخر مسألة العميان والزمني وقال : الغلة للمساكين لا لهما. ولو وقف على قراء القرآن والفقراء فالوقف باطل. وذكر هلال الوقف على الزمني المنقطعين صحيح . وقال المشايخ : الوقف على معلم المسجد الذي يعلم الصبيان غير صحيح. وقيل: يصح لأن الفقر غالب فيهم. قال شمس الأئمة فعلى هذا إذا وقف على طلبة علم بلدة كذا يجوز لأن الفقر غالب فيهم فكان الاسم منبئاً عن الحاجة والحاصل أنه متى ذكر مصرفاً فيه نص على الفقراء والحاجة فالوقف صحيح يحصون أم لا. وقوله يحصون» إشارة إلى أن التأبيد ليس بشرط ومتى ذكر مصرفاً يستوي فيه الغني والفقير إن كانوا يحصون صح بطريق التمليك، وإن كانوا لا يحصون فهو باطل إلا أن يكون في لفظه ما يدل على الحاجة كاليتامي فحينئذ إن كانوا يحصون فالأغنياء والفقراء سواء، وإن لا يحصون فالوقف صحيح ويصرف إلى فقرائهم لا إلى أغنيائهم، وكذا لو وقف على الزمني فهو على فقرائهم. وفي الفتاوي : لو وقف على الخصاف
Then he said: Whenever he mentions a place of need in a way that is perpetual, it suffices him from mentioning sadaqah. Similarly, [if he makes a waqf] for wayfarers or the chronically ill, then it is for the poor among them. In al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziyya, Abu Hanifa said: If he endows wealth for building bridges, or for repairing roads, or for digging graves, or for making water stations, or for purchasing shrouds for poor Muslims, it is not valid, unlike the waqf for mosques due to customary practice of the second but not the first. A waqf on the poor of Makkah or the poor of a known village: if they are not countable, it is valid during life and after death because it is perpetual; if they are countable, it is valid after death because it is a will, and a will for a countable group is valid, and when they become extinct it becomes inheritance from them; but during life it is not valid. A waqf of his land for the maintenance of endowed copies of the Quran is not valid because there is no custom for it. A waqf on his ummahat awlad and his slaves: the waqf is void according to Hilal. In al-Fatawa: A waqf on his ummahat awlad except that one who marries has no share; if her husband divorces her, her fallen right does not return unless the waqif made an exception and said 'whoever is divorced also has a portion of the waqf.' Al-Khassaf mentioned: He said, 'This land of mine is sadaqah, endowed to Allah, upon the people, or upon the sons of Adam, or upon the people of Baghdad forever, and when they become extinct then upon the poor, or upon the blind, or upon the chronically ill' — the waqf is void. He mentioned in another place the issue of the blind and the chronically ill, and said: The produce is for the poor, not for them. If one makes a waqf on reciters of the Quran and the poor, the waqf is void. Hilal mentioned that a waqf on the chronically ill who are confined is valid. The mashayikh said: A waqf on the teacher of the mosque who teaches children is not valid. It was said: It is valid because poverty is prevalent among them. Shams al-A'imma said: On this basis, if one makes a waqf on students of a certain town, it is valid because poverty is prevalent among them, so the name indicates need. The summary: Whenever he mentions a channel of disbursement that contains a text regarding the poor and need, the waqf is valid whether they are countable or not. His saying 'countable' is an indication that perpetuity is not a condition. And whenever he mentions a channel of disbursement that includes both rich and poor: if they are countable, it is valid by way of ownership; if they are not countable, it is void unless there is in his wording what indicates need, like 'orphans' — then if they are countable, the rich and poor are equal; if they are not countable, the waqf is valid and is paid to their poor, not to their rich. Similarly, if he makes a waqf on the chronically ill, it is for their poor. In al-Fatawa: If he makes a waqf on al-Khassaf...
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings (furu') regarding the validity of waqf based on the nature of the beneficiary or purpose. The key principle is that waqf must be perpetual; therefore, beneficiaries must be either unlimited in number or inherently poor (since poverty is an enduring condition). The text begins by stating that mentioning a 'place of need' (e.g., the poor) suffices for perpetuity without explicit ta'bid. For example, waqf for wayfarers or the chronically ill is valid, with the produce going to the poor among them. Abu Hanifa rules that waqf for public utilities like bridges, roads, graves, water stations, and shrouds is invalid, unlike mosques, due to custom ('urf). Waqf on the poor of a city is valid if they are not countable, as it is perpetual; if countable, it is treated as a will (valid only after death). Waqf on copies of the Quran is invalid due to lack of custom. Waqf on one's ummahat awlad and slaves is void according to Hilal, with a conditional exception in al-Fatawa. al-Khassaf discusses cases where the beneficiary is too broad (e.g., all people) or includes the blind/chronically ill without specifying poverty; such waqfs are void. However, if the beneficiary is described in a way that indicates need (like orphans or students where poverty is prevalent), the waqf is valid and goes to the poor among them. The concluding summary distinguishes between waqf for a class that includes both rich and poor: if countable, it is a valid ownership-based waqf but if not countable, it is void unless there is an indication of need (like 'orphans'), in which case it goes to the poor only.

Key Terms
يحصون (yuhsawna): They are countable; i.e., limited in number.
الزمني المنقطعين (al-zamana al-munqati'een): The confined chronically ill; those who are homebound.
اليتامي (al-yatama): Orphans; a category that indicates need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a waqf for 'the blind' without specifying poverty?
A) Valid, because the blind are in need.
B) Void, because the produce goes to the poor, not the blind.
C) Valid only after death.
D) Valid if they are not countable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf, if the beneficiary is countable and without need indication, it is a will; if uncountable, it is void unless need is indicated.

The Principle of Need-Based Class in Waqf
Establishes the principle that mentioning a need-based class suffices, with examples and distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that mentioning a need-based class in a perpetual manner suffices for waqf validity, and apply it to identify valid and invalid endowments.

Core Idea
A waqf for a need-based class is valid without the word 'charity', provided the class is perpetual and the name implies need.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a critical refinement in Hanafi waqf law. While a waqf for a specific group (like 'the poor of Mecca') is valid, the requirement for countability and the need to mention 'charity' depend on whether the group is defined by need. The mashayikh extend this to groups where poverty is prevalent, like students of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
The author states that mentioning a 'place of need' (mawdi' al-haja) in a perpetual manner suffices for the validity of the waqf, without needing the word 'charity' (sadaqa).
This principle is the core refinement—need-based classes imply charity by their nature.
2
Apply to Examples
Apply this to wayfarers and the chronically ill: the waqf is for the poor among them, even though the word 'poor' is not explicitly used.
The class 'wayfarers' or 'chronically ill' inherently points to need.
3
Contrast with Non-Need-Based Endowments
Abu Hanifa held endowments for bridges, roads, graves, water stations, and shrouds invalid, unlike mosques, due to custom. The distinction lies in whether the purpose is customarily supported by charity.
This shows that need-based classes are treated differently from utilitarian projects.
4
Understand the Countability Rule
For the poor of a known village, if they are not countable (i.e., a large indefinite number), the waqf is valid both during life and after death. If they are countable, it is valid after death as a will, but not during life as a waqf.
Countability affects the binding nature during the endower's lifetime.
5
Extend to Prevalent Poverty
Shams al-A'imma validates waqf for students of knowledge because poverty is prevalent among them, so the name implies need.
This extension relies on the concept of 'ghalabat al-faqr' (prevalence of poverty) to indicate need.

Source Text

Then he said: Whenever he mentions a place of need in a perpetual manner, it suffices him from mentioning charity. And similarly for wayfarers or the chronically ill, and it is for the poor among them. In al-Khulasa and al-Bazzaziya, Abu Hanifa said: If he endows property for building bridges, or repairing roads, or digging graves, or making water stations, or buying shrouds for poor Muslims, it is not valid, contrary to endowment for mosques due to custom of the second without the first. Endowment for the poor of Mecca or a known village: if they are not countable, it is valid in life and after death because it is perpetual; if they are countable, it is valid after death because it is a will, and a will to a countable group is valid, so when they become extinct it becomes inheritance from them; but during life it is not valid. Endowment of his land for maintaining endowed copies of the Quran is not valid because there is no custom in it. Endowment for his ummahat awlad and slaves: the endowment is invalid according to Hilal. In al-Fatawa: Endowment for ummahat awlad except that whoever marries has nothing, and if her husband divorces her, her dropped right does not return unless the endower exempted and said 'whoever is divorced also has a share from the endowment.' Al-Khassaf mentioned: He said 'My this land is a charity endowed perpetually to Allah for the people, or for the children of Adam, or for the people of Baghdad forever, then when they become extinct then for the poor, or for the blind, or for the chronically ill' - the endowment is invalid. And he mentioned in another place the issue of the blind and chronically ill and said: The produce is for the poor, not for them. And if he endows on reciters of Quran and the poor, the endowment is invalid. Hilal mentioned that endowment for the chronically ill who are cut off (destitute) is valid. The mashayikh said: Endowment for the teacher of the mosque who teaches boys is not valid. And it is said: it is valid because poverty is prevalent among them. Shams al-A'imma said: Accordingly, if he endows on students of knowledge of such a town, it is valid because poverty is prevalent among them, so the name indicates need. The conclusion: Whenever he mentions a disbursement that has an explicit text on the poor and need, the endowment is valid whether they are countable or not.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq begins by stating a key principle in Hanafi waqf law: specifying a need-based class (like 'the poor') in a perpetual manner suffices, and there is no need to explicitly mention the word 'charity' (sadaqa). The commentator then provides examples: wayfarers, the chronically ill—the endowment is for the poor among them. He then contrasts this with Abu Hanifa's ruling that endowments for public utilities like bridges, roads, graves, water stations, or shrouds for the poor are invalid, unlike mosques, due to custom. The discussion then moves to endowments for specific groups: the poor of Mecca or a village, distinguishing between countable and uncountable groups, and between the endower's lifetime and after death. The passage also covers endowments for ummahat awlad, slaves, and other categories, citing various authorities like al-Khassaf, Hilal, and the mashayikh. The key point is that if the named class explicitly indicates need (like 'the poor'), the endowment is valid regardless of countability, because the name implies the need for charity.

Key Terms
يحصون (yuhsawun): Countable; the beneficiaries are limited in number and can be enumerated individually.
موضع الحاجة (mawdi' al-haja): A class defined by need, such as 'the poor' or 'orphans'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is invalid because students are not necessarily poor.
B) It is valid because poverty is prevalent among them.
C) It is valid only if the students are countable.
D) It is invalid unless explicitly stated for the poor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle is: when a waqf names a need-based class perpetually, it is valid without explicit charity wording, even if the class is countable or poverty is only prevalent.

Implication of 'Sadaqa' in Waqf: Perpetuity and the Poor
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the implications of 'sadaqa' in waqf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that saying 'sadaqa' alone suffices for a valid waqf, and explain why perpetuity and the poor are implied.

Core Idea
The word 'sadaqa' in waqf implies perpetuity and the poor — no extra wording needed.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator clarifies the legal effect of using the word 'sadaqa' (charity) in a waqf declaration. The key question is whether additional words like 'forever' (abadan) or 'for the poor' are necessary. You will learn the consensus of the majority Hanafi masters and understand the reasoning behind their position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The passage asks whether saying 'sadaqa mawqufa' without explicitly mentioning perpetuity or the poor creates a valid waqf.
2
State the majority Hanafi view
Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Hilal al-Ra'y hold that it is valid and the waqf is for the poor, because 'sadaqa' naturally implies the poor and perpetuity follows from their persistence.
Trust in the linguistic implication of the term.
3
Contrast with the minority view
Yusuf ibn Khalid al-Samti requires explicit mention of 'forever' and a final destination for the poor.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Analyze the Hanafi reasoning
The majority argue: (1) Charity is originally for the poor, so no need to mention them. (2) The poor never cease to exist, so perpetuity is implied – no need to say 'forever'.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the essence of sadaqa.
5
Apply the ruling
If someone says 'This is sadaqa mawqufa' without more, the waqf is valid and is for the poor. The ruling does not change even if the poor are not specified.
This is mufta bihi according to the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله يحصون» إشارة إلى أن التأبيد ليس بشرط ومتى ذكر مصرفاً يستوي فيه الغني والفقير إن كانوا يحصون صح بطريق التمليك، وإن كانوا لا يحصون فهو باطل إلا أن يكون في لفظه ما يدل على الحاجة كاليتامي فحينئذ إن كانوا يحصون فالأغنياء والفقراء سواء، وإن لا يحصون فالوقف صحيح ويصرف إلى فقرائهم لا إلى أغنيائهم، وكذا لو وقف على الزمني فهو على فقرائهم. وفي الفتاوي : لو وقف على الخصاف يوسف : يجوز ويكون وقفاً على المساكين. ولو قال موقوفة صدقة أو صدقة موقوفة ولم يزد على ذلك جاز في قول أبي يوسف ومحمد وهلال الرائي ويكون وقفاً على الفقراء. وقال يوسف بن خالد السمتي: لا يجوز ما لم يقل وآخرها للمساكين أبداً. والصحيح قول أصحابنا لأن محل الصدقة في الأصل الفقراء فلا يحتاج إلى ذكر الفقراء ولا انقطاع للفقراء فلا يحتاج إلى ذكر الأبد أيضاً اهـ. فهذا صريح في أن لفظ صدقة تأبيد.
And his statement 'they are countable' is an indication that perpetuity is not a condition. And whenever he mentions a disbursement in which the rich and poor are equal, if they are countable, it is valid by way of ownership; and if they are not countable, it is invalid unless his wording indicates need, like 'orphans', then if they are countable, the rich and poor are equal; and if they are not countable, the endowment is valid and is disbursed to their poor, not to their rich. Similarly, if he endows on the chronically ill, it is for their poor. And in al-Fatawa: If he endows on al-Khassaf, Yusuf says: It is permissible and becomes an endowment for the poor. And if he says 'endowed charity' or 'charity endowed' and does not add anything else, it is permissible according to Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Hilal al-Ra'y, and it becomes an endowment for the poor. Yusuf ibn Khalid al-Samti said: It is not permissible unless he says 'and its end is for the poor forever.' The correct view is the statement of our companions because the place of charity originally is the poor, so there is no need to mention the poor, and there is no cessation of the poor, so there is no need to mention 'forever' either. Thus this is explicit that the word 'charity' implies perpetuity.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on waqf beneficiaries. It first clarifies that the Quranic share ('yuhsawun') in the matn indicates that perpetuity (ta'bid) is not a condition for validity; rather, it depends on whether the group is countable and whether the wording indicates need. If the group includes both rich and poor and is countable, the waqf is valid as a transfer of ownership (tamlik). If uncountable, it is invalid unless the wording implies need (e.g., 'orphans'), in which case it is valid and goes to the poor. The passage then reports an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Hilal hold that saying 'endowed charity' (sadaqa mawqufa) suffices to make a valid waqf for the poor, without needing to add 'forever'. Yusuf ibn Khalid al-Samti disagrees, requiring explicit perpetuity and a final destination for the poor. The commentator declares the correct view is that of our companions (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Hilal) because 'charity' originally means for the poor, and the poor never cease to exist, so 'forever' is implied.

Key Terms
صدقة (sadaqa): In this context, it is used as a term for waqf, implying perpetuity and for the poor.
تأبيد (ta'bid): Perpetuity; the requirement that a waqf be permanent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It is valid only if the land is in the possession of the poor.
D) It is a gift, not a waqf.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the word 'sadaqa' in a waqf declaration suffices to imply perpetuity and the poor, without needing to add 'forever' explicitly.
