Waqf for Specific Needs: When Mentioning 'Need' Suffices and the Shift in Fatwa
Commentary on matn phrases about waqf validity and fatwa evolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why mentioning a need (like 'the poor') substitutes for the word 'charity' in a waqf statement, and state the later fatwa that permits waqf for shrouds, jihad, and graves.

Core Idea
In waqf, specifying the need implies charity; later Hanafi fatwa permits endowments for shrouds and graves despite earlier invalidity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies key phrases in Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the validity of waqf. The commentator explains that when the endower mentions a need-based class (e.g., 'the poor'), the word 'charity' need not be explicitly stated because the need itself conveys the charitable intent. He also notes a significant shift in fatwa: while Abu Hanifa originally ruled that waqf for buying shrouds, jihad, and digging graves was invalid, later muftis have given fatwa with permissibility. This illustrates how fatwa can evolve with custom (urf) in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn phrase and its interpretation
The matn says 'يكفيه عن ذكر الصدقة' meaning it suffices to omit the word 'charity' when the purpose (e.g., for the poor) is mentioned.
The commentator says 'أي يكفي الاقتصار على لفظ وقفت عن ذكر الصدقة معه'.
2
Understand the rationale
Because mentioning the place of need (e.g., 'the poor') is equivalent to mentioning charity in meaning.
The phrase 'في معنى ذكر الصدقة' is key.
3
Recognize the shift in fatwa for specific purposes
The text says 'سيأتي أنه يفتي بالجواز' for buying shrouds, warfare, and digging graves, indicating a change from earlier invalidity.
The earlier ruling is referenced as 'على خلاف ما تقدم'.
4
Distinguish between valid and invalid waqf purposes
Waqf for 'people of Baghdad' is invalid because it includes both rich and poor and is uncountable; but specific purposes (shrouds, jihad, graves) are now permissible.
The invalidity is due to lack of specificity and inclusion of non-needy.
5
Note the authority weight
The later fatwa (yufata bi al-jawaz) represents the mufta bihi position from later Hanafi masters (fatawa mutakhkhirin).
This shift shows the evolution of fiqh law based on custom and need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (يكفيه عن ذكر (الصدقة أي يكفي الاقتصار على لفظ وقفت عن ذكر الصدقة معه لأن ذكر موضع الحاجة كالفقراء مثلاً في معنى ذكر الصدقة. قوله: (أو لشراء الأكفان الخ) سيأتي أنه يفتي بالجواز. قوله : (فالوقف باطل) لأنه للغني والفقير وهم لا يحصون وإنما الجهاد والغزو أو في أكفان الموتى أو حفر القبور يفتى بالجواز، وهذا على خلاف ما تقدم.
His statement: '(it suffices him from mentioning) charity' means that confining himself to the word 'I have endowed' suffices him from mentioning charity along with it, because mentioning the place of need, like the poor for example, is in the meaning of mentioning charity. His statement: '(or for buying shrouds, etc.)' will come that he gives fatwa with permissibility. His statement: '(the endowment is invalid)' because it is for the rich and poor, and they are not countable; however, by consensus, jihad, warfare, buying shrouds for the dead, or digging graves, fatwa is given with permissibility, contrary to what preceded.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's explanation of specific phrases from the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq). The first phrase 'يكفيه عن ذكر الصدقة' means that when the endower mentions a need-based class (e.g., 'the poor'), the word 'charity' (sadaqa) does not need to be explicitly mentioned because the need itself implies charity. The second phrase 'أو لشراء الأكفان' refers to a previous statement that buying shrouds was declared invalid by Abu Hanifa; the commentator notes that later fatwa has shifted to permissibility. The third phrase explains that the invalidity of certain endowments (like for 'people of Baghdad') is because they include both rich and poor and are uncountable; however, for purposes like jihad, shrouds, and digging graves, the fatwa has changed to permissibility, differing from the earlier ruling of Abu Hanifa. This illustrates the evolution of fatwa and the role of custom (urf) in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
يفتي بالجواز (yufta bi al-jawaz): Fatwa is given with permissibility; the later relied-upon opinion.
ذكر موضع الحاجة (dhikr mawdi' al-hajah): Mentioning the place of need (e.g., the poor) in the waqf statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about the ruling on waqf for buying shrouds?
A) It remains invalid according to Abu Hanifa.
B) Fatwa is now given with permissibility.
C) It is only valid if mentioned as 'sadaqa'.
D) It is valid only for the poor among Muslims.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf law, mentioning a specific need (like the poor) exempts from explicitly saying 'charity', and later fatwa permits endowments for shrouds, jihad, and graves, overriding earlier invalidity.

Ruling on Waqf of Real Estate with Appurtenances
Sharh explaining the permissibility of waqf of land with its fixtures and movables

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on waqf of real estate with movable fixtures, and apply the distinction between property that enters automatically and property that does not.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, waqf of land automatically includes permanent fixtures but not seasonal produce.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explains the validity of waqf of real estate together with its movable appurtenances like cattle and workers. The commentator builds on the matn, citing the practice of the Companions and the reasoning of Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad. You will learn not only the ruling but also the detailed list of what is included and what is excluded in a waqf of land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Locate the matn statement: 'And the waqf of real estate with its cattle and its workers is valid.' Recognize that this is the primary ruling being commented on.
2
Trace the proof for waqf of real estate
The commentator cites the practice of the Companions as proof (dalīl). Understand that this is a Sunnah-based proof (thubut zannī, dalāla qat‘ī).
The proof is implicit; the text assumes its validity.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on movables
Imām Abū Ḥanīfa generally prohibits independent waqf of movables. Abū Yūsuf allows it when the movables are fixtures (taba‘an) to land. Muḥammad agrees because he already permits independent waqf of some movables.
The principle: something established as a fixture may be included even if it would not be valid on its own.
4
Apply the qiyas (analogy)
The reasoning uses qiyās jaliyy: just as water rights are included in a sale without mention, and buildings are included in a waqf, so are cattle and tools when they are necessary for the land's purpose.
The ‘illa (effective cause) is being a fixture (taba‘) in achieving the intended purpose.
5
Catalog the inclusions and exclusions
From the text, list items that automatically enter the waqf (trees, buildings, water rights, cattle, slaves) and those that do not (crops, seasonal plants, fruit at time of waqf). Note the special ruling for existing fruit (qiyās vs. istiḥsān).
Permanence is the key distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وصح وقف العقار ببقره واكرته) أما العقار منفرداً فلأن جماعة من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم وقفوه، وأما جواز وقف المنقول تبعاً للعقار فإطلاق قول الإمام أنه لا يجوز وقف المنقول يمنعه كوقفه قصداً. وقال أبو يوسف: إذا وقف ضيعة ببقرها وأكرتها وهم عبيده، جاز، وكذلك في سائر آلات الحراثة لأنها تبع للأرض في تحصيل ما هو المقصود، وقد يثبت من الحكم تبعاً ما لا يحصل مقصوداً كالشرب في البيع والبناء في الوقف ومحمد معه فيه لأنه لما جاز إفراد بعض المنقول بالوقف عنده فلأن يجوز الوقف فيه تبعاً أولى والعقار الأرض مبنية كانت أو غير مبنية؛ كذا في فتح القدير: وفي القاموس: العقار الضيعة كالعقرى بالضم ويدخل الشرب والطريق والمسيل والشجر والبناء في وقف الأرض بلا ذكر، ولا يدخل الزرع والرياحين والخلاف والآس والثمر والبقل والطرفاء وما في الأجمة من حطب والورد والياسمين وورق الحناء والقطن والباذنجان. وأما الأصول التي تبقى والشجر الذي لا يقطع إلا بعد عامين أو أكثر فإنها تدخل تبعاً والبقر والعبيد بلا ذكر. ولا تخل الأشجار العظام والأبنية فيما إذا جعل أرضه أو داره مقبرة وتكون له ولورثته من مده، ولو وقف أرضه بحقوقها وجميع ما فيها ومنها وعلى الشجرة ثمرة قائمة يوم الوقف، قال هلال في القياس: تكون الثمرة له ولا تدخل في الوقف، وفي الاستحسان يلزمه التصدق بها على الفقراء على وجه النذر لا على وجه الوقف. ولو وقف داراً
His statement: (And the waqf of real estate with its cattle and its workers is valid). As for real estate alone, it is because a group of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, made waqf of it. As for the permissibility of waqf of movable property as a fixture to real estate, the absolute statement of the Imam (Abu Hanifa) that waqf of movable property is not allowed prevents it like [if it were] waqf intentionally. And Abu Yusuf said: If he makes waqf of an estate with its cattle and its workers who are his slaves, it is valid, and similarly for other agricultural tools, because they are fixtures to the land in achieving the intended purpose, and from the ruling, something may be established as a fixture that would not be established intentionally, like water rights in a sale and buildings in a waqf. And Muhammad agreed with him, because since he permits the independent waqf of some movable property, then permitting its waqf as a fixture is even more appropriate. And real estate is land, whether built upon or not; so in Fath al-Qadir: and in al-Qamus: real estate (al-'iqar) is the estate (al-day'ah) like al-'uqra with damm. And the water right, the road, the watercourse, the trees, and the building enter in the waqf of land without mention. And the crops, the aromatic plants, the [willow?], the myrtle, the fruit, the vegetables, the tamarisk, what is in the thicket of firewood, the rose, the jasmine, the henna leaves, cotton, and eggplant do not enter. As for the roots that remain and the tree that is not cut except after two years or more, they enter as fixtures, and the cattle and slaves without mention. And the large trees and buildings are not left when he makes his land or his house a cemetery, and it belongs to him and his heirs from his [???]. If he makes waqf of his land with its rights and all that is in it and from it, and on the tree there is a fruit existing on the day of waqf, Hilal said in qiyas: the fruit belongs to him and does not enter the waqf, and in istihsan, he is obliged to give it in charity to the poor on the basis of a vow (nazr), not on the basis of waqf. And if he makes waqf of a house…
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn statement: 'And the waqf of real estate with its cattle and its workers is valid.' The commentator first establishes the validity of waqf of real estate alone by citing the practice of the Companions, which serves as proof. Then he addresses the issue of movable property (cattle, slaves, tools) included as fixtures (taba') to the land. The default position of Imam Abu Hanifa is that independent waqf of movable property is not allowed, but he is silent on fixtures. Abu Yusuf explicitly permits waqf of an estate with its cattle and workers because they are essential for cultivation. Muhammad agrees because he already allows independent waqf of some movables. The commentary then cites Fath al-Qadir and al-Qamus to define 'real estate' and lists items that automatically enter the waqf of land (water rights, roads, trees, buildings) and those that do not (crops, fresh produce). A key distinction is between permanent fixtures (trees that remain, buildings) and seasonal items. The passage also discusses two scenarios: when land is made a cemetery (the donor retains ownership of trees and buildings) and when there are existing fruits on trees at the time of waqf. On the latter, Hilal presents qiyas (analogy): the fruit belongs to the donor, but istihsan (juristic preference) requires him to give it in charity as a vow (nazr), not as part of the waqf. The commentary emphasizes that the waqf of land includes all necessary fixtures by default, following Hanafi juristic reasoning.

Key Terms
العقار (al-'iqar): Real estate, specifically land including buildings, defined as 'الضيعة' (estate) in the Qamus.
تبعاً (taba'an): As a fixture; something attached or necessary to the primary property, included automatically without mention.
المنقول (al-manqul): Movable property, which Imam Abu Hanifa generally does not permit to be made waqf independently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following items automatically enters the waqf of land without being mentioned?
A) Crops and vegetables
B) Trees and buildings
C) Cotton and henna leaves
D) Fruits existing at the time of waqf

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf, land includes its permanent fixtures by default; movable property is included only when necessary for the land's purpose, and seasonal items are excluded.

Six Categories of Waqf Formulations in Hanafi Fiqh
Commentary enumerating six types of waqf formulations and their validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the six waqf formulations and state their validity according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The validity of a waqf depends on its wording: explicit reference to Allah or the poor ensures perpetuity; specifics without charitable destination are disputed.

This passage from al-Muhit classifies waqf wordings into six types, each with distinct rulings. Understanding these categories is essential for drafting valid endowments and for grasping the nuances of Hanafi waqf law.

Guided Path
1
Read the six categories
Go through each of the six formulations listed in the passage. Note the exact wording used for each type.
2
Determine validity
For each category, identify whether the waqf becomes valid (jāʾiz) or not, and note any conditions like perpetuity (taʾbīd).
3
Note ikhtilāf
Pay attention to differences between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybānī regarding the third and sixth categories.
The mukhtār (preferred) position is indicated.
4
Relate to custom
Observe how custom (ʿurf) influences the ruling for the maintenance of a mosque (sixth type).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : لا يجوز الوقف على الأغنياء وحدهم، ولو شرط بعدهم للفقراء جاز، ولو وقف على معين ولو يذكر آخره للفقراء فهو على ستة : الأول هذه صدقة الله أو موقوفة الله أو صدقة موقوفة الله تعالى صار وقفاً على الفقراء ذكر الأبد أو لا الثاني موقوفة صدقة على وجوه البر أو الخير أو اليتامى جاز مؤبداً كالفقراء والثالث موقوفة على فلان بعينه أو على ولدي أو فقراء قرابتي لا يصير عند أبي يوسف. والرابع . وقفاً عند محمد ويصح صدقة موقوفة على فلان جاز عند الكل. الخامس وقف على المساكين جاز بلا ذكر الأبد السادس على العمارة لمسجد بعينه ولم يذكر آخره للمساكين قيل عند محمد لا يجوز وعند أبي يوسف يجوز، وقيل اتفاقاً وهو المختار لمكان العرف .اهـ.
And in al-Muhit: It is not permissible to endow solely upon the rich, and if he stipulates after them for the poor, it is permissible. And if he endows upon a specific person and does not mention the end for the poor, then it is of six types: First: 'this is a charity of Allah' or 'endowed of Allah' or 'a charity endowed of Allah the Exalted' – it becomes a waqf for the poor whether he mentioned perpetuity or not. Second: 'endowed as charity upon charitable causes or goodness or orphans' – it is valid perpetually like the poor. Third: 'endowed upon Zayd specifically' or 'upon my child' or 'poor of my relatives' – it does not become a waqf according to Abu Yusuf. Fourth: ... [unclear] according to Muhammad, and 'a charity endowed upon Zayd' is valid by consensus. Fifth: 'endowment upon the poor' – valid without mentioning perpetuity. Sixth: 'for the upkeep of a specific mosque' without mentioning the end for the poor – it is said that according to Muhammad it is not valid, and according to Abu Yusuf it is valid, and it is said by agreement, and that is the chosen view due to custom. End quote.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit enumerates six distinct formulations for a waqf and their legal consequences. The first category uses wording explicitly linking to Allah, which automatically makes it a perpetual waqf for the poor. The second uses charitable wording without specifying a person, also perpetual. The third is a specific endowment without a final charitable destination; according to Abu Yusuf it does not become a valid waqf, though Muhammad disagrees in some interpretations. The fourth involves 'sadaqa mawqufa' wording on a specific person, which is valid by consensus because 'sadaqa' implies perpetuity. The fifth is general for the poor and needs no explicit perpetuity. The sixth involves maintenance of a mosque; the preferred view allows it due to custom. The commentary later notes that the third and fourth categories generated ikhtilaf among the Hanafi imams.

Key Terms
وقف على معين (waqf 'ala mu'ayyan): An endowment made to a specific individual or group, with or without a final charitable destination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is valid by consensus.
B) It does not become a waqf according to Abu Yusuf.
C) It is valid only if perpetuity is mentioned.
D) It is invalid according to all.

Key Takeaway
A waqf's wording determines its validity; explicit mention of perpetuity or a charitable destination is required, and custom can affect the ruling.

Waqf of Real Estate with Accessories: Validity and Dependency Rules
Ruling with proof on waqf dependency, comparing Abu Hanifa and his two disciples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on waqf of real estate with accessories, identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and apply the taba'iyyah principle to determine which items are included in a waqf of land without explicit mention.

Core Idea
Movables essential to land's cultivation enter the waqf by dependency, even if not independently endowable – a ruling established by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the validity of endowing real estate along with its essential accessories, such as cows and plowmen. It clarifies the intra-Hanafi disagreement: while Imam Abu Hanifa generally prohibits waqf of movables, his two disciples permit them as dependencies when they serve the primary property. The passage then provides a detailed list of what is included by dependency and what must be explicitly mentioned, based on the principle of taba'iyyah (dependency). Understanding these rules is crucial for drafting valid waqf deeds and avoiding common errors.

Guided Path
1
State the Core Ruling
Identify the matn's ruling: 'And it is valid to endow real estate with its cows and its plowmen.' Note that the validity of waqf of land alone is established by the practice of the Companions.
The Arabic: (وصح وقف العقار ببقره واكرته)
2
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Contrast Abu Hanifa's absolute prohibition of waqf of movables (even as dependency) with the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, who permit it when the movables are dependent on the land for cultivation.
Abu Hanifa's general rule is found in other texts; here the disciples' view is upheld.
3
Understand the Taba'iyyah Principle
The principle that a dependent item (tabi') acquires the ruling of the principal (matbu') for the purpose of achieving the intended objective. The passage draws analogy (qiyas) to the inclusion of water rights in a sale and buildings in a waqf.
تابع: ما يثبت تبعاً ما لا يثبت مقصوداً
4
Classify Items Included vs. Excluded
Go through the list: included automatically are water rights, pathways, drainage, trees, buildings, cows, plowmen, and millstones. Excluded unless mentioned: crops, fruits, certain plants, and non-essential movable items.
Perennial roots and trees not cut for two years+ are included; annual crops are not.
5
Apply to Specific Cases
Consider variations: existing fruit at time of waqf (qiyas: belongs to donor; istihsan: charity), doves and bees in a house, sugarcane, rose trees, etc. Also note the invalidity if the founder excludes trees from the land waqf.
The waqf of land with trees excluding trees makes the subject-matter unknown (jahalah), invalidating the waqf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وصح وقف العقار ببقره واكرته) أما العقار منفرداً فلأن جماعة من الصحابة رضي الله عنهم وقفوه، وأما جواز وقف المنقول تبعاً للعقار فإطلاق قول الإمام أنه لا يجوز وقف المنقول يمنعه كوقفه قصداً. وقال أبو يوسف: إذا وقف ضيعة ببقرها وأكرتها وهم عبيده ،جاز، وكذلك في سائر آلات الحراثة لأنها تبع للأرض في تحصيل ما هو المقصود، وقد يثبت من الحكم تبعاً ما لا يحصل مقصوداً كالشرب في البيع والبناء في الوقف ومحمد معه فيه لأنه لما جاز إفراد بعض المنقول بالوقف عنده فلأن يجوز الوقف فيه تبعاً أولى والعقار الأرض مبنية كانت أو غير مبنية ؛ كذا في فتح القدير : وفي القاموس : العقار الضيعة كالعقرى بالضم ويدخل الشرب والطريق والمسيل والشجر والبناء في وقف الأرض بلا ذكر، ولا يدخل الزرع والرياحين والخلاف والآس والثمر والبقل والطرفاء وما في الأجمة من حطب والورد والياسمين وورق الحناء والقطن والباذنجان. وأما الأصول التي تبقى والشجر الذي لا يقطع إلا بعد عامين أو أكثر فإنها تدخل تبعاً والبقر والعبيد بلا ذكر . ولا تخل الأشجار العظام والأبنية فيما إذا جعل أرضه أو داره مقبرة وتكون له ولورثته من مده، ولو وقف أرضه بحقوقها وجميع ما فيها ومنها وعلى الشجرة ثمرة قائمة يوم الوقف، قال هلال في القياس : تكون الثمرة له ولا تدخل في الوقف، وفي الاستحسان يلزمه التصدق بها على الفقراء على وجه النذر لا على وجه الوقف. ولو وقف داراً ما فيها وفيها حمامات يطرن أو بيتاً وفيها كورات عسل يدخل الحمام والنحل تبعاً بجميع للدار والعسل؛ كذا في الإسعاف والحاصل أن الوقف كالبيع لا يدخل فيهما الزرع والثمر إلا بالذكر، وفي الإقرار بأرض في يده لرجل وفيها ثمرة قائمة كانت الثمرة للمقر له بالأرض إذا كانت متصلة بالأرض وفي الهبة قال هلال لا تدخل الثمرة في الهبة والهبة باطلة لمكان الشيوع. وقال أبو جعفر : وهذا الحكم في الهبة إنما عرف بقول هلال ليس فيها رواية ظاهرة عن أصحابنا، وفي رهن الأرض يدخل الشجر والكرم والبناء والزرع والثمر في قول أصحابنا ويجوز الرهن ; كذا في الخانية. وفيها : لو وقفها بحقوقها فالثمرة التي تكون على الأشجار تدخل في الوقف وفي البيع لا تدخل ولو قال بكل قليل وكثير تدخل في البيع اهـ. وفي الظهيرية وقصب السكر لا يدخل، وشجر الورد والياسمين يدخل، والرحى تدخل في وقف الضيعة ورحى الماء ورحى اليد في ذلك سواء، وكذلك الدواليب تدخل والدوالي لا تدخل، وفي وقف الحمام تدخل قدور الحمام وفي وقف الحانوت يدخل ما كان يدخل في بيعها وخوابي الدباسين وقدور الدباغين لا تدخل سواء كانت في البناء أو لم تكن اهـ. وفي المحيط وقف أرضاً فيها أشجار واستثنى الأشجار لا يجوز الوقف لأنه صار مستثنياً للأشجار بمواضعها فيصير الداخل تحت الوقف مجهولاً اهـ. والأكرة ـ بفتح الهمزة والكاف ـ الحراثون من أكرت الأرض حرثتها واسم الفاعل أكار للمبالغة والجمع أكرة كأنه جمع آکر وزان كفرة جمع كافر; كذا في المصباح. وفي العناية : الأكرة جمع أكار وهو الزراع كأنها جمع آكر تقديراً.
His saying: (And it is valid to endow real estate with its cows and its plowmen) As for real estate alone, because a group of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, endowed it. As for the permissibility of endowing movable property as a dependency of real estate, the absolute statement of the Imam (Abu Hanifa) that endowing movable property is not permissible prevents it, like endowing it independently. Abu Yusuf said: If he endows an estate with its cows and its plowmen, and they are his slaves, it is valid, and similarly for all other tools of cultivation, because they are dependent on the land in achieving the intended purpose, and sometimes a ruling is established for a dependency that would not be achieved if intended independently, like the right to water in a sale and buildings in a waqf. Muhammad agrees with him in this because since it is permissible to endow some movables independently according to him, then it is more fitting that it be permissible for them as a dependency. Real estate is land, whether built or not; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Qamus: al-'iqar is the estate (day'a), like al-'uqara (with damm). The right to water, pathway, drainage, trees, and buildings are included in the waqf of land without mention. Crops, aromatic plants, willows, myrtle, fruit, legumes, tamarisk, firewood from thickets, roses, jasmine, henna leaves, cotton, and eggplants are not included. As for the roots that remain and trees that are not cut except after two years or more, they are included as a dependency, and cows and slaves without mention. Large trees and buildings are not excluded when he makes his land or house a cemetery, and it remains for him and his heirs from his time. If he endows his land with its rights and everything in it and on it, and on the tree is existing fruit on the day of endowment, Hilal said in qiyas: The fruit belongs to him and is not included in the waqf. In istihsan, he must give it in charity to the poor as a form of nadhr (vow), not as a waqf. If he endows a house with what is in it, and in it are doves that fly or a room, and in it are honeycombs, the doves and bees are included as a dependency, all for the house and honey; thus in al-Is'af. The conclusion is that the waqf is like a sale: crops and fruits are not included except by mention. In iqrar (acknowledgment) of land in his hand for a man, and on it is existing fruit, the fruit belongs to the one acknowledged for the land if it is attached to the land. In hibah (gift), Hilal said: The fruit is not included in the gift, and the gift is invalid due to common ownership. Abu Ja'far said: This ruling in hibah is only known by Hilal's statement; there is no apparent narration from our companions. In rahn (mortgage) of land, trees, vines, buildings, crops, and fruit are included according to our companions, and the mortgage is valid; thus in al-Khaniyya. And in it: If he endows it with its rights, then the fruit that is on the trees is included in the waqf, but in a sale it is not included. If he says 'with every little and much,' it is included in the sale. End quote. In al-Zahiriyya: Sugarcane is not included; rose and jasmine trees are included. The millstone is included in the waqf of the estate, whether water-mill or hand-mill are the same. Likewise, waterwheels are included, but trellises are not. In the waqf of a bathhouse, its cauldrons are included. In the waqf of a shop, what would be included in its sale is included; the barrels of molasses-makers and cauldrons of tanners are not included, whether they are in the building or not. End quote. In al-Muhit: If he endows land with trees and excludes the trees, the waqf is invalid because he has excluded the trees with their locations, making what is included in the waqf unknown. End quote. Al-akara (with fatha on hamza and kaaf) are the plowmen; from 'akartu al-arda' meaning I plowed it; the nomen agentis is akkaar for intensification; the plural is akara as if it is the plural of aakir, like kafara is plural of kaafir; thus in al-Misbah. In al-Inaya: al-akara is plural of akkaar, which is the cultivator, as if it is the plural of aakir by estimation.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn's statement that it is valid to endow real estate with its cows and plowmen. It first establishes that waqf of real estate alone is valid based on the practice of the Companions. Then it addresses the inclusion of movables as dependencies: Imam Abu Hanifa generally does not permit waqf of movables independently, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow them as dependencies of land, drawing an analogy (qiyas) to the inclusion of water rights in a sale and buildings in a waqf. The passage then specifies what is included in a waqf of land without explicit mention: water rights, pathways, drainage, trees, and buildings are included. Crops, fruits, and certain plants are not included unless mentioned. Perennial roots and trees that are not cut for two years or more are included as dependencies. Cows and slaves used for cultivation are included. There is a special rule for fruit existing at the time of waqf: according to qiyas it belongs to the donor, but istihsan requires him to give it in charity as a vow. The passage also compares waqf to sale, iqrar, hibah, and rahn regarding inclusion of fruits and crops, noting differences. It further discusses specific items like doves, bees, millstones, waterwheels, and cauldrons. Finally, if the donor excludes trees from land waqf, the waqf is invalid due to uncertainty. The lexical notes explain 'akara' as plowmen.

Key Terms
تبعية (taba'iyyah): The legal principle that something is included as a dependent part of the main property, even if not explicitly mentioned, because it serves the primary purpose.
العقار (al-'iqar): Real estate, specifically land, whether built or not; includes the estate (day'a).
الأكرة (al-akara): Plowmen or cultivators, plural of akkaar or aakir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on existing fruit on trees at the time of waqf of the land?
A) It is included in the waqf by consensus.
B) According to qiyas it belongs to the donor, but istihsan requires charity.
C) It automatically becomes part of the waqf.
D) The waqf is invalid if fruit exists.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, movables essential to the use of endowed land enter the waqf automatically by dependency, based on the position of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Specification in Waqf: Validity vs. Testimony
Ruling on the condition of specification for validity and testimony in waqf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the conditions for validity of waqf and the requirements for testimony regarding waqf, and apply the Hanafi position that specification (tahdid) is not required for validity.

Core Idea
Waqf is valid if the property is known; specification of boundaries is only needed for testimony in a dispute.

Many students assume that a waqf requires a detailed legal description of boundaries for its validity. This lesson clarifies that the Hanafi school only requires the endowed property to be known (ma'lum) for validity, while specification (tahdid) becomes relevant only when testimony is needed to prove the waqf. We will explore the key distinction and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling on validity
Identify the ruling: the author (al-Musannif) did not require specification for validity; the condition is that the property is known.
The ruling is from the matn, so it's the relied-upon position.
2
Distinguish validity from testimony
Note that the passage separates the conditions for the waqf to be valid from the conditions for testimony to be accepted in court.
Validity: property known. Testimony: property must be specified to avoid ambiguity.
3
Examine the examples from al-Khulasa
Review each of the three testimony cases: (1) boundaries unknown but land known – testimony valid; (2) boundaries known but location unknown – testimony valid; (3) land known but not specified – testimony rejected because he may have another land.
The key factor is whether the testimony identifies a specific property without confusion.
4
Reconcile with Fath al-Qadir
Fath al-Qadir appears to require specification, but the commentator clarifies that this requirement is only for accepting testimony, not for the validity of the waqf itself.
The apparent contradiction is resolved by the distinction between sihha (validity) and thubut (proof).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يشترط المصنف لصحة وقف العقار تحديده وإنما الشرط كون الموقوف معلوماً ولذا قال في الخلاصة : ولو قالا أشهدنا على أرضه أنه وقفها وهو فيها ولم يذكر لنا حدودها جازت شهادتهما لأنهما شهدا على وقف أرض بعينها إلا أنهما لا جيران الحدود فلم يتمكن الخلل في شهادتهما، ولو شهدا على أن الواقف وقف أرضه وذكر حدودها ولكنا لا نعرف تلك الأرض في أنها في أي مكان جازت شهادتهما ويكلف المدعي إقامة البينة أن الأرض التي يدعيها هذه الأرض، ولو شهدا أنه وقف أرضه ولم يحددها لنا ولكنا نعرف أرضه لا تقبل شهادتهما لعل للواقف أرضاً أخرى، وكذا لو قالا لا نعرف له أرضاً أخرى لا تقبل شهادتهما لعل للواقف أرضاً أخرى وهما لا يعلمان اهـ. وظاهر ما في فتح القدير اشتراط تحديدها فإنه قال : إذا كانت الدار مشهورة معروفة صح وقفها وإن لم تحدد استغناء بشهرتها عن تحديدها .اهـ. ولا يخفى ما فيه إنما ذلك الشرط لقبول الشهادة بوقفيتها
And the author (al-Musannif) did not require specification (tahdid) for the validity of waqf of real estate; rather the condition is that the endowed property be known (ma'lum). Hence he said in al-Khulasa: If two witnesses say, 'We testify regarding his land that he endowed it while he was in it and he did not mention its boundaries to us,' their testimony is valid because they testified to the endowment of a specific land, but they do not know the boundaries, so no defect occurs in their testimony. If they testify that the waqif endowed his land and mentioned its boundaries, but we do not know that land in terms of its location, their testimony is valid, and the claimant is required to provide evidence that the land he claims is this land. If they testify that he endowed his land and did not specify it to us, but we know his land, their testimony is not accepted because perhaps the waqif has another land. Similarly, if they say, 'We do not know of another land for him,' their testimony is not accepted because perhaps the waqif has another land and they do not know it. End quote. The apparent meaning in Fath al-Qadir is that specification is required, for he said: If the house is well-known and recognized, its waqf is valid even if not specified, due to being independent of specification by its fame. End quote. It is not hidden that this condition is only for acceptance of testimony regarding its being a waqf.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage clarifies the condition for the validity of waqf of real estate: it is not specification (tahdid/boundary description) but rather the property being known (ma'lum). The author of the matn did not require specification. The commentary from al-Khulasa provides examples for testimony: if witnesses know the land is his but not the boundaries, their testimony is valid; if they know boundaries but not the location, valid; but if they know his land but he did not specify, testimony is rejected because he might have another land. Fath al-Qadir appears to require specification, but the commentator resolves that this requirement is only for accepting testimony, not for the validity of the waqf itself. Thus, the distinction is between validity (sihha) and proof (thubut).

Key Terms
تحديد (tahdid): Specification or delineation of boundaries of the endowed property.
الموقوف معلوم (al-mawquf ma'lum): The endowed property must be known (identifiable) to the parties, even if boundaries are not specified.
صحة (sihha): Validity; the legal soundness of the waqf.
ثبوت (thubut): Proof or establishment in court; here used for testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Khulasa cited in the passage, if two witnesses testify that someone endowed his land but did not specify its boundaries, and they know the land, is their testimony accepted?
A) Yes, because the land is known.
B) No, because boundaries are required for testimony.
C) Yes, but only if the waqif is present.
D) No, because they might be mistaken.

Key Takeaway
Specification (tahdid) is not a condition for the validity of waqf; it only matters for testimony.

6 min
General Rule: Waqf Like Sale – Produce Excluded Without Mention
Establishes the general rule that waqf is analogous to sale for inclusion of produce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi rule that crops and fruits are not included in a waqf of land unless explicitly mentioned, and identify the exception when the endowment is made 'with its rights'.

Core Idea
Waqf is like sale: crops and fruits require explicit mention to be included.

When a piece of land is endowed, what happens to the crops or fruits growing on it? The Hanafi school draws a clear analogy to sale. Just as in a sale of land the produce does not automatically transfer to the buyer, so too in a waqf the produce remains outside the endowment unless the waqif specifically states otherwise. This principle ensures that the waqif's intention is clearly documented and prevents future disputes. The passage also contrasts this rule with the treatment of produce in gift and mortgage transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Analogy
The passage explicitly states 'الوقف كالبيع' (waqf is like sale). Understand that this analogy is the foundation of the ruling.
The analogy is based on the idea that both involve transfer of property, so similar rules apply regarding what is included.
2
Derive the Default Rule
In a sale, crops and fruits attached to the land are not automatically included; they remain the seller's property unless the contract specifies them. By analogy, in a waqf, they are also excluded unless the waqif mentions them.
The default rule is non-inclusion of produce.
3
Examine the Exception
If the waqif adds the phrase 'with its rights' (بحقوقها), the fruits on the trees at the time of endowment enter the waqf. This is an exception that broadens the endowment.
The phrase 'حقوق' (rights) is understood by custom to include the attached produce.
4
Contrast with Other Transactions
The passage also discusses gift and mortgage. In gift, including fruit can make the gift invalid due to joint ownership (shuyu'), whereas in mortgage, all produce enters automatically. Compare these to understand the unique logic of waqf.
Waqf follows sale, not mortgage, because waqf is a permanent transfer like sale, not a temporary security like mortgage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما فيها وفيها حمامات يطرن أو بيتاً وفيها كورات عسل يدخل الحمام والنحل تبعاً بجميع للدار والعسل؛ كذا في الإسعاف والحاصل أن الوقف كالبيع لا يدخل فيهما الزرع والثمر إلا بالذكر، وفي الإقرار بأرض في يده لرجل وفيها ثمرة قائمة كانت الثمرة للمقر له بالأرض إذا كانت متصلة بالأرض وفي الهبة قال هلال لا تدخل الثمرة في الهبة والهبة باطلة لمكان الشيوع. وقال أبو جعفر : وهذا الحكم في الهبة إنما عرف بقول هلال ليس فيها رواية ظاهرة عن أصحابنا، وفي رهن الأرض يدخل الشجر والكرم والبناء والزرع والثمر في قول أصحابنا ويجوز الرهن ؛ كذا في الخانية. وفيها : لو وقفها بحقوقها فالثمرة التي تكون على الأشجار تدخل في الوقف وفي البيع لا تدخل ولو قال بكل قليل وكثير تدخل في البيع اهـ. وفي الظهيرية وقصب السكر لا يدخل، وشجر الورد والياسمين يدخل، والرحى تدخل في وقف الضيعة ورحى الماء ورحى اليد في ذلك سواء، وكذلك الدواليب تدخل والدوالي لا تدخل، وفي وقف الحمام تدخل قدور الحمام وفي وقف الحانوت يدخل ما كان يدخل في بيعها وخوابي الدباسين وقدور الدباغين لا تدخل سواء كانت في البناء أو لم تكن اهـ.
What is in it: if there are doves that fly or a house and there are honeycombs, the doves and bees enter as a follow-up (taba') with all for the house and honey; so in al-Is'af. The conclusion is that waqf is like sale: crops and fruits are not included except by mention. In the matter of confession of land in his hand for a man and there is fruit upon it standing, the fruit belongs to the one confessed for him with the land if the fruit is connected to the land. In gift, Hilal said the fruit does not enter the gift and the gift is invalid due to joint ownership (shuyu'). Abu Ja'far said: This ruling in gift is only known by the saying of Hilal; there is no manifest transmission from our companions. In mortgage of land, trees, vines, buildings, crops, and fruit enter according to our companions, and the mortgage is valid; so in al-Khaniyya. And in it: If he endowed it with its rights (bi-huquqiha), the fruit that is on the trees enters the waqf, and in sale it does not enter; and if he said 'with every small and great', it enters in sale. In al-Zahiriyya: sugarcane does not enter; rose and jasmine trees enter; the gristmill enters in the endowment of a farm (day'a), whether watermill or handmill; likewise the waterwheels (dawalib) enter, and the vines (dawali) do not enter. In the endowment of a bathhouse, its cauldrons enter; in the endowment of a shop, what enters in its sale enters; the jars of oil-pressers and the cauldrons of tanners do not enter, whether they are in the structure or not.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from multiple Hanafi sources regarding what automatically becomes part of a waqf of land or building. The general principle is that waqf is analogous to sale: crops and fruit growing on the land are not included in the waqf unless explicitly mentioned (except if the waqf is made 'with all its rights'). However, certain accessories like doves, bees, trees (such as rose and jasmine), millstones, waterwheels, and cauldrons of a bathhouse are considered followers (taba') of the property and are included without mention. The passage also notes differences in gift and mortgage. The Hanafi position is compared with opinions of Hilal and Abu Ja'far, but the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that waqf follows the rules of sale for crops and fruits, while accessories enter as taba' based on custom (urf).

Key Terms
تبعاً (taba'an): As followers; items that are automatically included as part of the property without explicit mention.
الشيوع (al-shuyu'): Joint ownership that makes a gift invalid if the gift is not separately identified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, if someone endows a piece of land with fruit-bearing trees, but does not mention the fruit, what is the ruling regarding the fruit?
A) The fruit automatically enters the waqf.
B) The fruit does not enter the waqf.
C) The fruit enters the waqf only if the land is a farm.
D) The fruit enters the waqf if the trees are explicitly included.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi waqf, crops and fruits are not automatically included; they require explicit mention or the phrase 'with its rights'.

Accessories (Taba') in Waqf – What Enters Without Mention
Lists specific items that are or are not included as accessories in waqf of various properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which items are considered accessories (taba') and automatically included in a waqf of land or building, and which require explicit mention.

Core Idea
Custom (urf) determines which items are accessories; physical attachment and function decide inclusion.

The previous lesson established that crops and fruits require explicit mention. But what about doves, beehives, mills, or cauldrons? The Hanafi school, drawing on custom (urf), treats certain items as 'accessories' (taba') that naturally belong to the property and thus enter the waqf without being named. This lesson explores the specific examples given in the passage for land, houses, bathhouses, shops, and farms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Concept of Taba'
Taba' means 'following': items that are so closely connected to the property that they are considered part of it by custom. The passage gives examples.
Custom (urf) is the key criterion, not just physical attachment.
2
Identify Examples of Items That Enter as Taba'
For land: doves living in the house, bees in beehives, rose and jasmine trees, gristmills (hand or water), waterwheels. For a bathhouse: cauldrons. These enter automatically.
Sugarcane does NOT enter – perhaps because it is considered a crop like grain.
3
Identify Items That Do NOT Enter
Items that are not considered integral: vines (dawali) are excluded from a farm; jars of oil-pressers and cauldrons of tanners do not enter even if built into the structure.
The ruling about oil jars and tanning cauldrons shows that even structural integration does not guarantee inclusion.
4
Apply the General Rule for Shops
The passage says: in a shop waqf, whatever enters in the sale of a shop enters in the waqf. This ties back to the analogy with sale.
Custom determines what is sold with a shop in the locality.

Waqf — Endowment

6 min
Specification (Tahdīd) in Waqf: Condition for Validity or Testimony?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tahdid in waqf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the conditions for validity of a waqf and the conditions for acceptance of testimony, and explain the Hanafi position that specification of boundaries is not required for validity.

Core Idea
Boundary specification is needed for testimony, not for the validity of a waqf.

After discussing the invalidity of endowing land while excluding trees (due to uncertainty), the passage turns to a more foundational question: must the boundaries of an endowed property be specified for the waqf to be valid? Hanafi scholars differ, but the later relied-upon view separates the validity of the waqf from the clarity of testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings in the passage
First, the ruling on endowing land with trees excluded is invalid (majhul). Second, the main dispute: is tahdid required for validity of waqf?
Note that the trees exclusion is a separate issue; our focus is the tahdid dispute.
2
State the Hanafi position according to the author (al-Kanz al-Daqa'iq)
The author did not stipulate specification; the condition is that the property be known (ma'lum). Fame (shuhra) suffices.
This is the basis for the later position.
3
Examine the conflicting view from al-Khassaf
Al-Khassaf reportedly said: 'The endowment is invalid unless the land is famous' – implying specification or fame is required for validity.
This appears to require tahdid for validity.
4
Understand Hilal's interpretation
Hilal argued that al-Khassaf's statement refers to the invalidity of testimony, not the endowment itself. The waqf is valid if the property is known; witnesses failing to specify boundaries only makes their testimony defective.
This reconciliation saves the waqf from being invalidated by witness failure.
5
Identify the tarjih (preferred view)
The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) sides with Hilal and the later scholars. The mufta bihi is that specification is not a condition for validity; the waqf stands if the property is known.
Thus, tahdid is only a condition for testimony, not for the waqf itself.

وغيرهما، وحكمهم على العموم حكم الأرقاء فليس له أن يزوج بنته بلا إذن. وفي البزازية : ولو زوج الحاكم جارية الوقف جاز وعبده لا يجوز ولو من أمة الوقف لأنه يلزمه المهر والنفقة اهـ . وظاهره أن المتولي لا أصلاً كذلك
In al-Muhit: If one endows land in which there are trees and excludes the trees, the endowment is not valid because he has excepted the trees in their places, thus what falls under the endowment becomes unknown. Al-Akrah (with fatha on hamza and kaf) means plowmen, from 'akartu al-arda' (I plowed the land); the active participle is akkar (intensive), plural akarah, as if it is plural of aakir, like kafarah plural of kafir; so in al-Misbah. In al-Inaya: al-akrah is the plural of akkar, meaning cultivator, as if it is plural of aakir by estimation. The author did not stipulate specification (tahdid) for the validity of endowment of real estate; rather the condition is that the endowed property be known. Hence in al-Khulasa: If two witnesses said 'We witnessed him endow his land while he was in it and he did not mention its boundaries to us', their testimony is accepted because they witnessed the endowment of a specific land, but they are not neighbors of the boundaries, so no defect occurs in their testimony. If they testified that the founder endowed his land and mentioned its boundaries but we do not know that land in terms of where it is, their testimony is accepted, and the claimant is required to bring evidence that the land he claims is this land. If they testified that he endowed his land and did not specify it for us, but we know his land, their testimony is not accepted because the founder might have another land. Similarly, if they said 'We do not know he has another land', their testimony is not accepted because the founder might have another land and they are unaware. The apparent of what is in Fath al-Qadir is the condition of specifying it, for he said: If the house is famous and known, its endowment is valid even if not specified, relying on its fame to dispense with specification. It is not hidden that this condition is only for acceptance of testimony of its endowment. The text of Nusus: Just as if one endows a farm and mentions what is in it of slaves, waterwheels, and plowing tools, they become endowments as followers. So his saying 'and he mentioned what is in it' indicates that they do not enter without mention, and that is the import of the text's statement 'And the endowment of real estate with its cattle and its plowmen is valid'. His saying 'it is not hidden' is an objection to Fath, and he explains it by his saying 'this condition is etc.' The gist is that the understanding as we have presented. In al-Qunyah: Endowment of a farm, he mentions the boundaries of the exempted items like cemeteries, roads, mosques, and public basins; then it is stated that it is necessary to mention the boundaries if possible, then it is stated that the endowment is not valid without specification. In Fath al-Qadir: Endowment of real estate for a mosque or school, he prepared a place for its construction before building it; the later scholars differed, and the correct view is permissibility, and its produce is spent on the poor until it is built; when built, the produce is returned to it, derived from the endowment of children of so-and-so when he has no children; they ruled it valid and its produce is spent on the poor until a child is born to so-and-so. The author indicated that slaves are validly endowed as followers of the farm, but he did not mention their rulings regarding marriage, injury, etc. Their general ruling is that of slaves: he may not marry his daughter without permission. In al-Bazzaziyya: If the judge marries a female slave of the endowment, it is permissible, but his male slave is not permissible even if from the female slave of the endowment because he incurs the dower and maintenance. Its apparent meaning is that the trustee (mutawalli) does not have authority except with the permission of the judge, and there is no difference between the judge and the sultan as in al-Khulasa. In al-Is'af: If one of them commits an injury, it is upon the trustee to do what is more expedient: pay ransom or hand him over. If he ransoms him for more than the value of the injury, he is a volunteer in the excess and must pay it from his own wealth; if the people of the endowment ransom him, they are volunteers, and the slave remains as he was regarding work in charity. In al-Bazzaziyya: The injury of a slave of the endowment is against the endowment's wealth. As for the ruling of injury upon him, in al-Bazzaziyya: If a slave of the endowment is killed intentionally, there is no retaliation upon him. It is not hidden that when retaliation is not required, his value is due as if he was killed by mistake, and the trustee buys a slave with it and becomes an endowment, just as if a mudabbar (slave freed upon master's death) is killed by mistake and the master takes his value, he buys a slave with it and becomes mudabbar. This is explicitly stated in al-Dhakhirah attributed to al-Khassaf. As for his maintenance, it is from the endowment's wealth even if the founder did not stipulate it. In al-Is'af: If he stipulated their maintenance from its produce, then one of them becomes ill, he deserves maintenance if he said 'on condition that injury occurs'. That... is from the statement of Fath where he restricted to the famous, that other than it is not valid unless specified, and there is a conflict with what is required for acceptance of testimony, not for validity of endowment. But it is not hidden that what is in al-Qunyah agrees with what is understood from Fath, and that being about testimony does not contradict this; ponder. In Awqaf al-Khassaf: I said: What do you say if two witnesses testify that he confessed before them that he endowed his land which is in such a place, and they said he did not specify it for us? He said: The endowment is invalid unless the land is famous such that its fame dispenses with its specification; if it is like that, I judge it to be an endowment. Then I saw in Anfa' al-Wasa'il after dividing the issue of specification into seven forms, he said: As for the third form, i.e., if he did not specify it and they do not know it, al-Khassaf said: the endowment is invalid unless it is famous, etc. Hilal said: The testimony is invalid. There is no doubt that what al-Khassaf said requires interpretation, and it is not permissible to act on its apparent meaning because specification is not required for the validity of the endowment in itself; rather it is valid by the founder's saying 'I endowed my house for such-and-such'. It is not permissible to invalidate the endowment merely by the witnesses saying 'he did not specify it for us and we do not know it and it is not famous'. So it is necessary to interpret al-Khassaf's statement 'the endowment is invalid' as meaning 'the testimony is invalid' as Hilal and others said. This is something that requires attention and vigilance in understanding it, to the end of what he said, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 335

Commentary
This passage is a combined discussion of two related issues: (1) the invalidity of endowing land with trees while excluding the trees, because it renders the subject unknown (majhul), and (2) whether specification of boundaries (tahdid) is required for the validity of a waqf. The passage begins with a ruling from al-Muhit that waqf with exclusion of trees is invalid. It then defines 'akarah (plowmen) and explains that the author (presumably al-Kanz al-Daqa'iq) did not require specification for validity, only that the property be known. The passage then presents a detailed discussion on the acceptance of testimony regarding a waqf, quoting from al-Khulasa, Fath al-Qadir, al-Qunyah, and ultimately a dispute between al-Khassaf and Hilal. The relied-upon position is that specification is not a condition for validity; rather it is a condition for the acceptance of testimony. The passage ends with a call to interpret al-Khassaf's statement accordingly. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Khassaf appears to require specification (or fame) for validity, while the later view (supported by Hilal and the commentator) treats it as a requirement for testimony only.

Key Terms
التحديد (al-tahdid): Specification of the boundaries (limits) of the property.
المشهورة (al-mashhurah): A famous property known by reputation, which suffices to identify it without boundaries.
الأكرة (al-akarah): Plowmen or farmers who work the land; plural of akkar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the effect if a person endows his land without specifying its boundaries, but the land is well-known in the area?
A) The waqf is invalid because boundaries are required.
B) The waqf is valid because the land is known by fame.
C) The waqf is valid only if the witnesses specify boundaries in their testimony.
D) The waqf is invalid unless the founder later specifies the boundaries.

Key Takeaway
Specification of boundaries is a condition for testimony, not for the validity of a waqf; the waqf is valid if the property is known.

Rulings on Slaves Included in a Waqf
Rulings on waqf slaves: marriage, injury, maintenance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings regarding marriage, injury, and maintenance of slaves that are part of a waqf, and explain the restrictions on the trustee's authority.

Core Idea
Trustee's authority over waqf slaves is limited; marriage requires judge's permission, injury compensated, maintenance obligatory.

This passage expands on the rulings for slaves that are part of a waqf (e.g., as followers of a farm). It covers three key areas: marriage of waqf slaves, liability for injuries caused by or upon them, and their maintenance. The Hanafi school treats them similarly to ordinary slaves but with specific restrictions on the trustee's powers, often requiring judicial oversight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three areas of rulings
The passage addresses marriage, injury (both caused by and upon the slave), and maintenance. First, categorize these areas to understand the scope.
2
Analyze the marriage ruling
Note that the trustee cannot marry a female waqf slave; only the judge can. For a male waqf slave, marriage to a female waqf slave is not permitted due to liability for dower and maintenance.
The restriction protects waqf assets from new obligations.
3
Analyze the injury rulings
If the slave causes injury, the trustee chooses ransom or handover. If the slave is intentionally killed, no qisas; instead, compensation is paid and used to buy a new waqf slave, analogous to a mudabbar slave.
The analogy with mudabbar reinforces the rule.
4
Analyze the maintenance ruling
Maintenance of waqf slaves is from waqf funds even if not stipulated by the founder. The cut-off text discusses a conditional scenario on stipulation.
This follows the principle that upkeep of waqf property is from its proceeds.

The author indicated that it is valid to endow slaves as followers of the farm, but he did not mention their rulings regarding marriage, injury, etc. Their general ruling is that of slaves: he may not marry his daughter without permission. In al-Bazzaziyya: If the judge marries a female slave of the endowment, it is permissible, but his male slave is not permissible even if from the female slave of the endowment because he incurs the dower and maintenance. Its apparent meaning is that the trustee (mutawalli) does not have authority except with the permission of the judge, and there is no difference between the judge and the sultan as in al-Khulasa. In al-Is'af: If one of them commits an injury, it is upon the trustee to do what is more expedient: pay ransom or hand him over. If he ransoms him for more than the value of the injury, he is a volunteer in the excess and must pay it from his own wealth; if the people of the endowment ransom him, they are volunteers, and the slave remains as he was regarding work in charity. In al-Bazzaziyya: The injury of a slave of the endowment is against the endowment's wealth. As for the ruling of injury upon him, in al-Bazzaziyya: If a slave of the endowment is killed intentionally, there is no retaliation upon him. It is not hidden that when retaliation is not required, his value is due as if he was killed by mistake, and the trustee buys a slave with it and becomes an endowment, just as if a mudabbar (slave freed upon master's death) is killed by mistake and the master takes his value, he buys a slave with it and becomes mudabbar. Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage deals with secondary rulings for slaves that are part of a waqf (e.g., as followers of a farm). It clarifies that these slaves are treated like ordinary slaves in terms of legal capacity, but the trustee's authority over marriage is restricted: the trustee cannot marry a female slave of the waqf; only the judge can. For a male slave, marriage is not permitted if it involves a female slave of the waqf due to liability for dower and maintenance. In case of injury caused by the slave, the trustee may either pay compensation or hand over the slave, and if he pays more than the actual injury value, he is a volunteer. If the slave is killed intentionally, no retaliation is due (since the slave is property, not a free person); instead, the value is compensated, and the trustee uses it to buy another slave for the waqf. Maintenance of the slave is obligatory from waqf income even if not stipulated by the founder. The passage draws an analogy with a mudabbar slave. This is a straightforward presentation of rulings; no ikhtilaf is mentioned.

Key Terms
المتولي (al-mutawalli): The trustee or administrator of the waqf, responsible for managing its assets and affairs.
الجناية (al-jinayah): An injurious act committed by the slave (e.g., causing harm to another person).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave of a waqf is killed intentionally by a third party, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The killer faces qiṣāṣ (retaliation) because the slave is a human being.
B) The killer must pay the value of the slave, and the trustee buys a new slave for the waqf.
C) The killer is exempt from any penalty because the slave is property.
D) The waqf is dissolved and the value is distributed to the beneficiaries.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, slaves attached to a waqf are governed by specific rules: the trustee's marriage authority is restricted, injuries are compensated from waqf funds with no qisas, and maintenance is obligatory from waqf income.

Waqf of Undivided Shares (Mushā') – Validity by Judicial Ruling
Ruling on waqf of undivided shares with judicial discretion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on waqf of undivided shares and explain why a judge's ruling makes it valid despite differing opinions.

Core Idea
Waqf of an undivided share is valid if a judge so rules, because it is a matter of ijtihad.

When property is jointly owned, can one endow only his share? The Hanafi school answers that it depends on a judge's ruling. This lesson unpacks the text from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq which clarifies that waqf of mushā' is a permissible subject of ijtihād, and a judge's decision is binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on waqf of mushā'
The text states: 'وزف المشاع قضى بجوازه' – the endowment of an undivided share is valid if a judge has ruled it so.
The ruling is conditional on a prior judicial decision.
2
Understand the reason: it is a matter of ijtihād
The validity of waqf of a share before division is a disputed issue among jurists. The Hanafi school considers it a 'فصل مجتهد فيه' (a matter open to ijtihād), meaning both validity and invalidity are possible based on differing interpretations.
This allows a judge to choose either position based on his own ijtihād or the preponderance of evidence.
3
Note the implication for the muqallid judge
A Hanafi judge (muqallid) may rule either way because there are two sound opinions in the school. The text says 'فإن للحنفي المقلد أن يحكم بصحة وقف المشاع وببطلانه لاختلاف الترجيح'.
This flexibility reflects the Hanafi recognition of legitimate ikhtilāf within the school itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : وقف ضيعة يذكر حدود المستثنيات من المقابر والطرقات والمساجد والحياض العامة، ثم رقم أنه لا بد من ذكر الحدود إن أمكن ثم رقم بأنه لا يصح الوقف بدون التحديد اهـ. وفي فتح القدير : وقف عقاراً على مسجد أو مدرسة هيأ مكاناً لبنائها قبل أن يبنيها، اختلف المتأخرون والصحيح الجواز وتصرف غلتها إلى الفقراء إلى أن تبنى، فإذا بنيت ردت إليها الغلة أخذاً من الوقف على أولاد فلان ولا أولاد له حكموا بصحته وتصرف غلته إلى الفقراء إلى أن يولد لفلان اهـ. وقد أفاد المصنف أن العبيد يصح وقفهم تبعاً للضيعة ولم يذكر أحكامهم في البقاء من التزويج والجناية وغيرهما، وحكمهم على العموم حكم الأرقاء فليس له أن يزوج بنته بلا إذن. وفي البزازية : ولو زوج الحاكم جارية الوقف جاز وعبده لا يجوز ولو من أمة الوقف لأنه يلزمه المهر والنفقة اهـ . وظاهره أن المتولي لا يملكه إلا بإذن القاضي ولا فرق بين القاضي والسلطان كما في الخلاصة. وفي الإسعاف: وإن أحد منهم جناية فعلى المتولي ما هو الأصلح من الدفع أو الفداء. ولو فداه بأكثر من أرش الجناية كان متطوعاً في الزائد فيضمنه من ماله وإن فداه أهل الوقف كانوا متطوعين ويبقى العبد على ما كان عليه من العمل في الصدقة .اهـ. وفي البزازية : وجناية عبد الوقف في مال الوقف، وأما حكم الجناية عليه ففي البزازية : قتل عبد الوقف عمداً لا قصاص عليه اهـ. ولا يخفى أنه إذا لم يجب القصاص تجب قيمته كما لو قتل خطأ ويشتري به المتولي عبداً ويصير وقفاً كما لو قتل المدبر خطأ وأخذ المولي قيمته فإنه يشتري بها عبداً ويصير مدبراً، وقد صرح به في الذخيرة معزياً إلى الخصاف. وأما نفقته فمن مال الوقف وإن لم يشترطه الواقف. وفي الإسعاف : لو شرط نفقتهم من غلتها ثم مرض بعضهم يستحق النفقة إن قال على أن يجري عليهم نفقاتهم من غلتها أبداً ما كانوا أحياء، وإن قال لعملهم فيها لا يجري شيء من الغلة على من تعطل منهم عن العمل، ولو باع العاجز واشترى بثمنه عبداً مكانه جاز اهـ . وقول المصنف أكرته دون عبيده فيه دليل على أن العبيد إنما يصح وقفهم تبعاً للضيعة لأجل زراعتها. وكذا قوله في الهداية لأنه تبع للأرض في تحصيل ما هو المقصود يدل على أنه لو وقف داراً فيها عبد وجعل العبد تبعاً لها لا يصح لأنه لا يصلح للتبعية لأن المقصود من الدار سكناها وهو يحصل بدون العبد بخلاف زراعة الأرض لا يحصل إلا بالحراثة، وأما وقف العبيد تبعاً للمدرسة والرباط فسيأتي أن بعض المشايخ جوزه وفي الولوالجية : رباط كثرت دوابه وعظمت مؤناتها هل للقيم أن يبيع شيئاً منها وينفق ثمنها في علفها أو مرمة الرباط؟ فهذا على وجهين : إن صارت البعض منها إلى حد لا يصلح لما ربط له كذلك لأنه لا يمكنه إمساكها وحفظها، وإن لم تصر بهذه الحالة ليس له ذلك إلا أنه يمسك في هذا الرباط مقدار ما يحتاج إليها ويربط ما زاد على ذلك في أدنى الرباط اهـ . قوله : ( ومشاع قضى بجوازه) أي وصح وقف المشاع إذا قضى بصحته لأنه قضاء في فصل مجتهد فيه ولا خلاف فيه، وإنما الخلاف فيما يحتمل القسمة قبل القضاء أطلق القاضي فشمل الحنفي وغيره فإن للحنفي المقلد أن يحكم بصحة وقف المشاع وببطلانه لاختلاف الترجيح وإذا كان في المسألة قولان مصححان فإنه يجوز القضاء والإفتاء بأحدهما كما صرحوا به قوله : (ومنقول فيه تعامل) أي وصح وقف المنقول مقصوداً إذا تعامل الناس وقفه، وأما الكراع والسلاح فلا خلاف فيه بين استحسان، والقياس أن لا يجوز لما بينا من قبل من أن التأبيد شرط وهو يتحقق فيه. وجه الاستحسان الآثار المشهورة فيه منها قوله عليه السلام «فأما خالد فقد حبس أدرعاً له في سبيل الله تعالى، وطلحة حبس أدرعاً له في سبيل الله تعالى» ويروى كراعه. وفي المجتبى والمراد من الكراع الخيل والحمير والبغال والإبل والثيران التي يحمل عليها، والمراد من السلاح ما يستعمل في الحرب ويكون معداً للقتال اهـ. وفي المصباح : درع الحديد مؤنثة في الأكثر ويصغر على دريع بغير هاء على قياس، ويجوز أن يكون التصغير على من ذكر وربما قيل دريعة بالهاء وجمعها أدرع ودروع وأدراع قال ابن الأثير : هي الزردية ذكره في الدال المهملة وأما ما سوى الكراع والسلاح فعند أبي يوسف لا يجوز وقفه لأن الشيخين وهو لغة .
And in al-Qunyah: Endowment of a land mentioning the boundaries of the excluded areas like cemeteries, roads, mosques, and public water troughs, then it is stated that it is necessary to mention the boundaries if possible, then it is stated that the endowment is not valid without specification. And in Fath al-Qadir: If one endows a land for a mosque or school and designates a place for its construction before building it, the later scholars differed and the correct view is permissibility, and its produce is given to the poor until it is built, and when it is built the produce is returned to it, analogously to the endowment for the children of so-and-so when he has no children – they ruled it valid and its produce is given to the poor until a child is born to so-and-so. And the author indicated that slaves are validly endowed ancillary to the land, but he did not mention their rulings regarding marriage, injury, etc. Their ruling in general is the same as that of slaves: he may not give his daughter in marriage without permission. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: If the judge marries a waqf slave-girl it is permissible, but as for his slave it is not permissible even if from a waqf slave-girl because he is liable for the dower and maintenance. Its apparent meaning is that the trustee does not have that authority except with the judge's permission, and there is no difference between the judge and the sultan as in al-Khulasa. And in al-Is'af: If one of them commits an injury, the trustee should do what is most beneficial: either surrender him or ransom. If he ransoms him for more than the compensation for the injury, he is a volunteer for the excess and is liable for it from his own wealth; if the people of the waqf ransom him, they are volunteers and the slave remains as he was working for charity. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: The injury of a waqf slave is against the waqf property. As for the ruling on injury against him: In al-Bazzaziyyah: If a waqf slave is killed intentionally, there is no retaliation. It is not hidden that if retaliation is not obligatory, his value is due as if he were killed by mistake, and the trustee buys a slave with it and it becomes waqf, just as if a mudabbar slave is killed by mistake and his master takes his value, he buys a slave with it and it becomes mudabbar, as stated in al-Dhakhira quoting al-Khassaf. And his maintenance is from the waqf property even if the endower did not stipulate it. And in al-Is'af: If he stipulated their maintenance from its produce, then some of them become sick, they deserve maintenance if he said 'on condition that their maintenance runs from its produce forever as long as they are alive'; but if he said 'for their work', nothing of the produce goes to those of them who are unable to work. And if he sells the disabled one and buys a slave with its price in his place, it is permissible. And the author's statement 'his plough oxen' without mentioning his slaves indicates that slaves are only validly endowed as ancillary to the land for its cultivation. Similarly his statement in al-Hidayah 'since it is ancillary to the land in achieving the intended purpose' indicates that if one endows a house containing a slave and makes the slave ancillary to it, it is not valid because he is not suitable for being ancillary, since the purpose of the house is dwelling which is achieved without the slave, unlike cultivation of land which is not achieved except through ploughing. As for endowment of slaves as ancillary to a school or ribat, it will come that some shaykhs permitted it. And in al-Walwalijiyyah: A ribat has many animals and its expenses become great – may the superintendent sell some of them and spend the price on their fodder or repair of the ribat? This has two aspects: if some of them have reached a state unfit for what they were tied for, then he may, because he cannot keep and preserve them; if they have not reached this state, he may not, except that he keeps in this ribat the amount he needs and ties the surplus in a lesser ribat. His statement: 'and a share when judgment is passed on its permissibility' means the endowment of a share is valid if a judge rules it valid, because it is a ruling in a matter open to ijtihad. There is no disagreement on this; rather the disagreement is about what can be divided before the ruling. The wording 'judge' is general and includes the Hanafi and others, for the Hanafi muqallid may rule either for its validity or invalidity due to differing preferences. And if there are two sound opinions on the issue, it is permissible to rule and give fatwa by either, as they explicitly stated. His statement: 'and movable property in which custom exists' means the endowment of movable property intended as such is valid if people customarily endow it. As for war-horses and weapons, there is no disagreement on them due to istihsan. Analogy would say it is not valid because perpetuity is a condition and it is not realized in them. The basis of istihsan is the well-known traditions among them, including his saying (peace be upon him): 'As for Khalid, he has detained armor for the sake of Allah, and Talha has detained armor for the sake of Allah,' and it is also narrated 'his war-horse'. And in al-Mujtaba: What is meant by 'war-horse' is horses, donkeys, mules, camels, and oxen which are ridden; and what is meant by 'weapons' is what is used in war and prepared for fighting. And in al-Misbah: 'Dir' (armor) of iron is feminine in most cases and its diminutive is 'duray' without ta' marbuta by analogy, and it may be diminutive according to the masculine, and sometimes 'duray' with ta'. Its plural forms are adru', duru', and adra'. Ibn al-Athir said: It is the mail coat, mentioned under the letter dal. As for [movable property] other than war-horses and weapons, according to Abu Yusuf it is not valid to endow them because the two shaykhs... and it is a language.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion of the Kanz al-Daqa'iq text. It addresses several key issues in Hanafi waqf law. The commentary first discusses the requirement of boundary specification for a land being endowed. While some sources like al-Qunyah state that specification is necessary, Fath al-Qadir clarifies that the dispute is about testimony, not validity. The correct position is that the judge may rule based on reputation of the land. The passage then discusses the ruling of endowing property intended for a mosque or school before it is built: the later scholars consider it valid, with the produce given to the poor until construction is complete. Next, the rulings of ancillaries (slaves and animals) in waqf are detailed. The author of Kanz indicates that slaves are only validly endowed as ancillaries to agricultural land, not to a house, because they are necessary for cultivation. The commentary provides detailed rules on their marriage, injuries, and maintenance. For example, the trustee may not marry a waqf slave-girl without the judge's permission; if a waqf slave is intentionally killed, there is no retaliation but his value must be paid and used to buy a replacement; maintenance of waqf slaves is from the waqf property even if not stipulated. The passage then addresses the endowment of shares (mushaa'): it is valid if a judge rules accordingly because it is a matter of ijtihad. Finally, it discusses the endowment of movable property (manqool). While analogy (qiyas) would prohibit it due to the requirement of perpetuity, istihsan permits it based on the practice of the Companions, particularly the hadith of Khalid and Talha detaining armor and horses in the path of Allah. Abu Yusuf only permitted war-horses and weapons, but the relied-upon position (as per the text) is that endowment of any movable property is valid if customarily practiced. This passage thus clarifies the Hanafi position that the validity of waqf depends on the intention of perpetuity and the usability of items in a way that can be perpetual, and custom and necessity play a role in determining validity.

Key Terms
المشاع (al-mushā'): An undivided share in jointly owned property, such as half of a house.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for a waqf of an undivided share (mushā') to be valid?
A) It must be divided first among co-owners.
B) A judge must rule it valid.
C) It must be for a charitable purpose only.
D) It must involve land, not movable property.

Key Takeaway
Waqf of an undivided share is valid if a judge rules it so, reflecting the Hanafi recognition of ijtihād in such matters.

Module 9 · Lesson 92

Waqf — Endowment
intermediate
10 min
Waqf of Movable Property (Manqool) – Validity by Custom and Istihsān
Ruling on waqf of movable property with istihsān and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates waqf of movable property via istihsān and identify the conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
Waqf of movable property is valid by custom and istihsān, despite qiyās requiring perpetuity.

Can one endow a horse, a set of books, or a piece of machinery? While analogy (qiyās) suggests such items cannot be permanently tied up, the Hanafi school permits it based on istihsān and established custom. This lesson explores the textual evidence from the hadith of Khalid and Talha and the limits of this ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule: waqf of movables is valid if customary
The text says: 'أي وصح وقف المنقول مقصوداً إذا تعامل الناس وقفه' – endowment of movable property intended as such is valid if people customarily endow it.
Custom (ta'āmul) is a key element; without custom, it may not be valid.
2
Understand the exception: war-horses and weapons are valid by consensus (istihsān)
The text explicitly excludes 'الكراع والسلاح' (war-horses and weapons) from the custom requirement because there is clear evidence and consensus on their permissibility via istihsān.
This exception is based on the hadith of Khalid and Talha.
3
Contrast with qiyās: why analogy would reject movable waqf
Analogy (qiyās) would invalidate movable waqf because perpetuity (ta'bīd) is a condition for waqf, and movables are perishable or consumable. The text states: 'والقياس أن لا يجوز لما بينا من قبل من أن التأبيد شرط وهو يتحقق فيه'.
The condition of perpetuity is fulfilled by the continuity of use (e.g., a horse for jihad) not necessarily the physical item itself.
4
Identify the basis of istihsān: the hadith and practice of Companions
The text cites the hadith: 'فأما خالد فقد حبس أدرعاً له في سبيل الله تعالى، وطلحة حبس أدرعاً له في سبيل الله تعالى' – the Companions Khalid and Talha endowed armor and horses.
The word 'حبس' (detained) is the same as 'waqf'. This shows that movables can be endowed for a perpetual purpose.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf's restriction
Abu Yusuf restricts valid movable waqf to war-horses and weapons only. The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) extends to any movable property that is customarily endowed.
The later authorities (mutakhkhirūn) adopted the wider view based on ta'āmul.
